Chapter on What Invalidates Pre-emption. Look, because he is a partner before division, not after, and the discussion is about after it. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Or he surrendered his pre-emption, then it is rescinded by an option of inspection, or condition, or defect by a judgment) meaning if the pre-emptor surrenders the pre-emption, then it is returned to the seller by an option of inspection or condition, no matter how, or it is sold by a judge's judgment, pre-emption is not obligatory therein because it is a rescission from all aspects, so it cannot be made a new contract; thus, his old ownership returns to him, and pre-emption is obligatory in a new creation, not in continuation and remaining as it was. There is no difference in that whether the rescission is before or after possession. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: No pre-emption in division or option of inspection... He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is obligatory if it is rescinded without a judgment or by mutual cancellation) meaning pre-emption becomes obligatory if the buyer returns it due to a defect without a judgment or by mutual cancellation of the sale. Zufar said: It is not obligatory because his pre-emption was invalidated by the surrender, and the return due to a defect without a judgment is an iqalah, and iqalah is a rescission due to their intent, and the consideration is the intent of the contracting parties. We said: Iqalah is a cause for ownership by mutual consent like a sale, except that they intended rescission, so it is valid in what does not involve invalidating the right of a third party because they have authority over themselves, so it is a rescission in their regard and they have no authority over others, so it is a new sale regarding the pre-emptor, thus his right of pre-emption is renewed. The commentator said: The author of al-Hidayah said that his intent by the return due to a defect is the return after possession. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: This only holds according to the saying of Muhammad because selling real estate before possession is not valid according to him, as in the transmitted view; but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? actually two are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? the text says 'qawlahuma' meaning Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), its sale before possession is valid, so the mentioned condition does not benefit. And Allah knows best. Chapter on What Invalidates Pre-emption. Because the invalidation of something requires its prior existence, he mentioned what invalidates pre-emption after mentioning what establishes it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is from al-Bahr al-Raiq under the chapter on what invalidates shuf'a. It explains that if the shafi' surrenders his right of shuf'a and then the buyer rescinds the sale due to an option (khiyar al-ru'ya, khiyar al-shart, or khiyar al-'ayb) through a court judgment, the shuf'a does not become obligatory because the rescission is a complete annulment (faskh), so the property returns to the seller's ownership as it was; shuf'a is only established in a new contract, not in maintaining an existing ownership. This applies whether the rescission occurs before or after possession. The passage then discusses a related ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir about shuf'a not applying in division or khiyar al-ru'ya, clarifying that khiyar al-ru'ya can exist in division. Then it explains the exception: if the rescission is without court judgment (e.g., mutual agreement or iqalah), shuf'a becomes obligatory because the rescission is considered a new sale from the perspective of the shafi'. This leads to a dispute with Imam Zufar, who argues that shuf'a is already invalidated by surrender. The Hanafi response is that iqalah is a cause of ownership by mutual consent, resembling a sale; it is treated as a rescission between the parties but as a new sale regarding third parties like the shafi'. Thus, the shafi' gets a new right. The passage concludes with a note from al-Hidayah clarifying that this ruling is based on Imam Muhammad's view that selling real estate before possession is not valid; on the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the condition of 'after possession' would not matter.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, returning the property to its original owner.
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of the parties, treated as a new sale regarding third parties.
شفيع (shafī'): The party entitled to pre-emption, typically a co-owner or neighbor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a shafi' surrenders his right of pre-emption, and then the buyer and seller mutually cancel the sale (iqalah), what is the ruling?
A) He has no right because the surrender is final.
B) He has a new right of shuf'a because iqalah is treated as a new sale regarding third parties.
C) He only has a right if the cancellation is by court order.
D) He only has a right if the cancellation occurs after he has taken possession.

Key Takeaway
A surrendered shuf'a right revives if the sale is mutually cancelled (iqalah) because iqalah is a new sale for third parties.

Effect of Rescission on Surrendered Pre-emption Right
Ruling on surrender of shuf'a followed by rescission of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a surrendered right of pre-emption revives after rescission of the sale, and explain the Hanafi legal reasoning that treats iqaala as a new sale vis-à-vis third parties.

Core Idea
A surrendered pre-emption right revives only if the sale is rescinded without a court order or by mutual rescission (iqaala), treating it as a new contract for the pre-emptor.

Imagine you are a co-owner who forgoes your right to buy your neighbor's share. Later, the buyer and seller cancel their deal. Does your pre-emption right come back? The Hanafi school distinguishes between cancellations by court order and those done privately. This lesson unpacks the rule and the clever legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the event sequence
The pre-emptor surrenders his right, then the sale is rescinded. Identify which type of rescission occurs: court-ordered (due to option or defect) or without court (by mutual agreement or out-of-court defect rejection).
The ruling hinges on the nature of the rescission.
2
Apply the rule for court-ordered rescission
If rescission is by court due to option (inspection, condition, defect), the pre-emption does not revive. Reason: it is a pure rescission, not a new contract.
No difference whether rescission before or after possession.
3
Apply the rule for rescission without court
If rescission is by mutual agreement (iqaala) or out-of-court defect rejection, the pre-emption revives.
This is the majority view.
4
Analyze the rationale
Iqaala is considered a new sale regarding third parties (the pre-emptor) because the parties have authority over themselves but not over others. Thus, the pre-emptor gets a new opportunity.
The intention of the parties is relevant for themselves but not binding on third parties.
5
Study the intra-Hanafi difference
Zufar holds the surrender is permanent. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds revival. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
This disagreement is legitimate within the school.

باب ما تبطل به الشفعة

بين
قال
رحمه الله : (أو سلمت شفعته ثم ردت بخيار رؤية أو شرط أو عيب بقضاء) يعني إذا أسلم الشفيع الشفعة ثم ردت إلى البائع بخيار رؤية أو شرط كيفما كان أو بيعت بقضاء القاضي لا تجب الشفعة فيه لأنه فسخ من كل وجه فلا يمكن أن يجعل عقداً جديداً فعاد إليه قديم ملكه والشفعة تجب في الإنشاء لا في الاستمرار والبقاء على ما كان، ولا فرق في ذلك أن يكون الفسخ قبل القبض أو بعده، وفي الجامع الصغير ولا شفعة في قسمة ولا خيار رؤية بالجر معناه لا شفعة في الرد بخيار رؤية وليس معناه أن خيار الرؤية لا يثبت في القسمة لأن المذكور في كتاب القسمة أن خيار الرؤية يثبت في القسمة وخيار الشرط أيضاً لأن ثبوتها لخلل في الرضا بالعقد الذي لا ينعقد لازماً إلا بالرضا والقسمة منه لما فيها من معنى المبادلة، والمبادلة أغلب في غير الكيلي والوزني فيجوز فيه خيار الرؤية والشرط ولا يجوز في المكيل والموزون لأن الإقرار فيهما هو الغالب. وقال في الكافي : وصحح شمس الأئمة السرخسي أن خيار الرؤية لا يثبت في القسمة سواء كانت بقضاء أو رضاء؛ قاله المشايخ . وقلنا : لا فرق بين أن يكون الفسخ قبل القبض أو بعده ؛ كذا في العناية. ولا عبرة بقول من قال المراد بعد القبض لأنه لو كان هذا مراداً كان مناقضاً لقولهم في غير هذا المحل ولا فرق بين أن يكون قبل القبض أو بعده ؛ كذا في العناية .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (وتجب لو ردت بلا قضاء أو تقايلاً) يعني تجب الشفعة إن ردها المشتري بعيب بغير قضاء أو تقايلا البيع. وقال زفر : لا تجب لأن شفعته بطلت بالتسليم والرد بالعيب بغير قضاء إقالة والإقالة فسخ لقصدهما ذلك والعبرة بقصد العاقدين قلنا : الإقالة سبب للملك بتراضيهما كالبيع غير أنهما قصد الفسخ فيصح فيما لا يتضمن إبطال حق الغير لأن لهما ولاية على أنفسهما فيكون فسخاً في حقهما ولا ولاية لهما على غيرهما فيكون بيعاً جديداً في حق الشفيع فيتجدد له حق الشفعة. قال الشارح قال صاحب الهداية ومراده بالرد بالعيب الرد بعد القبض. قال رحمه الله : وهذا إنما يستقيم على قوله محمد لأن بيع العقار عنده قبل القبض لا يجوز كما في المنقول، وأما على قولهما يجوز بيعه قبل القبض فلا يفيد القيد المذكور والله أعلم.
Chapter of Pre-emption, Section on What Nullifies Pre-emption. It is rejected by option of inspection, condition, or defect by a court ruling, and it becomes obligatory if rejected without a court ruling or by mutual rescission. Section on What Nullifies Pre-emption. He considered because he is a partner before division, not after, and the discussion is after division. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Or he surrenders his pre-emption right, then it is rejected by option of inspection, condition, or defect by a court ruling) meaning: if the pre-emptor surrenders the pre-emption, then it is returned to the seller by option of inspection or condition, however it may be, or it is sold by the judge's ruling, the pre-emption is not obligatory therein because it is a rescission from every aspect, so it cannot be made a new contract, so his old ownership returns to him, and pre-emption is obligatory in initiation, not in continuation and remaining as it was. There is no difference in that whether the rescission is before or after possession. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: There is no pre-emption in division and no option of inspection by inclination (with jar), meaning: there is no pre-emption in the rejection by option of inspection, and it is not meant that the option of inspection is not established in division, because what is mentioned in the Book of Division is that the option of inspection is established in division, and also the option of condition, because their establishment is due to a defect in consent to the contract which is not concluded as binding except with consent, and division is part of it due to what it contains of the meaning of exchange. Exchange is more prevalent in non-measured and non-weighed items, so the option of inspection and condition are permissible therein, but not in measured and weighed items because declaration (iqrar) is predominant in them. It is said in al-Kafi: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi authenticated that the option of inspection is not established in division, whether by court or by consent; the mashayikh said that. And we said: There is no difference between the rescission being before or after possession; likewise in al-Inaya. No heed is paid to the saying of whoever said that the meaning is after possession, because if this were intended, it would contradict their statement in another place: no difference between before and after possession; likewise in al-Inaya. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is obligatory if it is rejected without a court ruling or by mutual rescission) meaning: pre-emption is obligatory if the buyer rejects it due to a defect without a court ruling or they mutually rescind the sale. Zufar said: It is not obligatory because his pre-emption right was voided by the surrender, and rejection due to a defect without a court ruling is an iqaala, and iqaala is a rescission because they both intend that, and consideration is given to the intention of the contracting parties. We said: Iqaala is a cause for ownership by mutual consent like a sale, except that they intend rescission, so it is valid in what does not involve invalidating the right of a third party, because they have authority over themselves, so it is a rescission regarding them, but they have no authority over others, so it is a new sale regarding the pre-emptor, thus his right of pre-emption is renewed. The commentator said: The author of al-Hidayah said, and his intention by rejection due to a defect is rejection after possession. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: This is only correct according to Muhammad's saying, because in his view selling real estate before possession is not permissible, as in movable property. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's other view?), it is permissible to sell before possession, so the mentioned condition is useless. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where a pre-emptor initially surrenders his right to pre-emption (shuf'a), but later the sale is rescinded. The ruling makes a crucial distinction based on how the rescission occurs. If the rescission is by court order due to an option (inspection, condition, defect), the original surrender stands, and the right of pre-emption does not revive. The reasoning is that a court-ordered rescission is a complete rescission, not a new contract, so the property returns to the seller without creating a new opportunity for pre-emption. However, if the rescission occurs without a court order, either by mutual agreement (iqaala) or by the buyer rejecting the property due to a defect out of court, then the right of pre-emption revives. The key principle is that iqaala is treated as a new sale vis-à-vis third parties like the pre-emptor, because the parties have authority over themselves but not over others. Therefore, the pre-emptor gets a new right. This is the position of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). The passage also records the dissenting view of Zufar, who argued that the surrender is permanent regardless of subsequent rescission. The text sides with the majority and provides the underlying rationale. Additionally, the passage clarifies the applicability of options of inspection and condition in the context of division (qisma), with specific rules for measured vs. unmeasured items. This underscores the Hanafi approach of treating legal fictions (like iqaala as a new sale) to protect the rights of third parties.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption — a right of a co-owner to purchase the share sold to a third party.
الإقالة (al-iqaala): Mutual rescission of a contract by the parties.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of inspection — the right to rescind a sale if the item is not as seen or described.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, when does a surrendered pre-emption right revive?
A) When the rescission is by court order.
B) When the rescission is without court order or by iqaala.
C) Never.
D) Only if the rescission occurs before possession.

Key Takeaway
A surrendered pre-emption right revives after rescission only if the rescission is not by court order—treating iqaala as a new sale for the pre-emptor.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
What Nullifies the Right of Pre-emption (Shuf'a)
Listings of actions that nullify the right of pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three main actions that nullify the right of pre-emption and explain the ruling on settlements and guarantees, including why consideration must be returned.

Core Idea
The right of pre-emption is lost by delay, acquiescence, or any settlement—consideration received must be returned.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'a) allows a co-owner to step into a sale, but it is a limited right that must be exercised promptly. Once the pre-emptor learns of the sale and is able to claim, any delay or act of surrender nullifies his right. This lesson covers the specific triggers of nullification, including settling for consideration (sulh) and the analogous case of guarantee (kafala).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Nullifying Actions
The text lists three actions: leaving al-muwathaba (rushing), leaving al-taqrir (affirmation/acquiescence), and settling (al-sulh) for consideration. The first two are forms of turning away (i'rad).
Also mentioned: death of the pre‑emptor nullifies as well.
2
Understand the Condition for Nullification
Nullification by muwathaba or taqrir requires knowledge of the sale and ability to claim (no prevention, not in prayer).
3
Analyze the Ruling on Settlement
If the pre‑emptor settles with the buyer for consideration, his right is nullified, but he must return the consideration because the right is not a commodity—it is a mere right to own.
The principle: one cannot sell or buy the right itself.
4
Note the Analogous Case of Guarantee (Kafala)
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: in one narration, dropping pre-emption by settling also nullifies a personal guarantee; in another, the guarantee remains. The correct view (al-asahh) is that both fall, but no money is due.
The distinction: in kafala the beneficiary did not receive any consideration, so the guarantee should not fall; the commentator resolves by saying both fall.
5
Recognize Extra Casual Surrender
Merely asking the buyer to assign the property at the same price (or bargaining) is considered a surrender and nullifies the right.
This is based on the principle that any indication of giving up the right is effective.

Source Text

باب ما تبطل به الشفعة
به لما كان بطلان الشيء يقتضي سابقة وجوده ذكر ما تبطل به الشفعة بعد ذكر ما تثبت به
وتبطل بترك المواثبة أو التقرير وبالصلح عن شفعته على عوض وعليه رده وبموت [؟] الشفعة. قال رحمه الله : ( وتبطل بترك المواثبة أو التقرير حين علم مع القدرة عليه بأن لم يمنعه أحد ولم يكن في الصلاة لأنها تبطل بالإعراض وترك الطلبين أو أحدهما مع القدرة إعراض على ما تقدم. والأصل في هذا الباب أن تسليم الشفعة قبل البيع وإن من ثبت له يصح الحق إذا أسقطه بعد ثبوته له سقط علم بثبوته له أو لم يعلم. وتعبير المؤلف بترك الطلب أولى من تعبير صاحب الهداية بترك الإشهاد لأنه يرد على صاحب الهداية أن الإشهاد ليس بشرط وترك ما ليس شرطاً في الشيء لا يبطله. وفي المحيط لو سلم الشفعة للوكيل صح وسقطت ويصح تعليق الإسقاط بشرط ولو قال سلمت لك إن اشتريت لنفسك لم تبطل إذا كان اشتراها لغيره، ولو قال لأجنبي سلمت شفعة هذا سقطت شفعته لأنه سلم مطلقاً فصرفناه إلى المشتري حملاً لكلام العاقل على الصحة. ولو قال سلمت لك لا الأجنبي بمعزل عن هذا العقد، ولو قال له أجنبي سلم للمشتري فقال سلمت لك استحساناً كأنه قال سلمت الشفعة للمشتري لشفاعتك . قال رحمه الله : (وبالصلح عن شفعته على عوض وعليه رده يعني تبطل الشفعة إذا صالح المشتري الشفيع على عوض وعلى الشفيع رد العوض لأن حق الشفيع ليس بمقرر في المحل وإنما هو مجرد حق التملك فلا يجوز أخذ العوض عنه، ولا يتعلق إسقاطه بالجائز من الشرط فيما إذا قال الشفيع أسقطت شفعتي فيما اشتريت على أن تسقط حصتك فيما اشتريت أو على أن لا تطلب الثمن مني لكونه ملائماً حتى لو تراضيا سقط حق كل واحد منهما، ومع هذا لا يتعلق إسقاط الشفعة بهذا الشرط بل يسقط بمجرد قوله أسقطت تحقق الشرط أو لم يتحقق فأولى أن لا يتعلق بالشرط الفاسد وهو شرط الاعتياض عن حق ليس بمال بل هو رشوة محضة فيصح الإسقاط ويبطل الشرط، وكذا إذا باع شفعته بمال لما بينا ونظير ما نحن فيه إذا قال للمخيرة اختاري بألف أو قال ألفين لامرأته اختاري ترك الفسخ بألف فاختارت سقط الخيار ولا يثبت المال والكفالة بالنفس في هذا بمنزلة الشفعة في رواية وفي أخرى لا تبطل الكفالة ولا يجب المال. قال في شرح الجامع الكبير : إذا لم يجب العوض يجب أن لا تبطل شفعته كما في الكفالة. والفرق أن حق الشفيع قد سقط بعوض معنى فإن الثمن سلم له والمكفول له لم يرض بسقوط حقه عن الكفيل بغير عوض ولم يحصل له بعوض معنى فإن الثمن سلم له عوضاً أصلاً فلا يسقط حقه الكفالة اهـ. قال الشارح والأصح أن الكفالة والشفعة يسقطان ولا يجب المال. قيد بقوله شفعته لأنه لو صالح على أخذ نصيب الدار بنصف الثمن يجوز، ولو صالح عن بيت بحصته من الثمن لا يجوز الصلح ولا تسقط شفعته لأنه لم يوجد منه Section on What Nullifies Pre-emption. Because the nullification of something necessitates its prior existence, he mentioned what nullifies pre-emption after mentioning what establishes it. It is nullified by leaving rushing (al-muwathaba) or affirmation (al-taqrir), and by settling concerning his pre-emption for a consideration, and upon him is its return, and by the death [؟] of the pre-emption. The author said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is nullified by leaving rushing or affirmation when he knows and is able, meaning no one prevents him and he is not in prayer, because it is nullified by turning away, and leaving the two demands or one of them with ability is a turning away as previously mentioned. The principle in this chapter is that surrendering pre-emption before the sale is valid, and if the right becomes established for someone, it falls when he drops it after its establishment, whether he knows it is established or not. The author's expression 'leaving the demand' is better than the author of al-Hidayah's expression 'leaving the testimony' because it responds to the author of al-Hidayah that testimony is not a condition, and leaving what is not a condition for a thing does not nullify it. In al-Muhit: If he surrenders the pre-emption to the agent, it is valid and falls, and the suspension of the dropping on a condition is valid. If he says, 'I surrender to you on condition that you buy for yourself,' it is not voided if he bought it for another. If he says to a stranger, 'I surrender the pre-emption of this,' his pre-emption falls because he surrendered absolutely, so we direct it to the buyer, carrying the speech of a rational person on validity. If he says, 'I surrender to you, not the stranger,' he is removed from this contract. If a stranger says to him, 'Surrender to the buyer,' and he says, 'I surrender to you,' it is istihsan, as if he said, 'I surrender the pre-emption to the buyer for your intercession.' The author said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by settling concerning his pre-emption for a consideration, and upon him is its return) means the pre-emption is nullified if the buyer settles with the pre-emptor for a consideration, and upon the pre-emptor is to return the consideration, because the right of the pre-emptor is not established in the property; rather it is merely a right of ownership, so it is not permissible to take consideration for it. And its dropping is not attached to the permissible condition, as when the pre-emptor says, 'I drop my pre-emption in what I bought on condition that you drop your share in what I bought' or 'that you do not demand the price from me' because it is fitting, so that if they mutually agree, the right of each falls. Despite this, the dropping of pre-emption is not attached to this condition; rather it falls by his mere saying 'I drop', whether the condition is realized or not. So it is more appropriate that it is not attached to a void condition, and it is the condition of taking compensation for a right that is not property, but it is pure bribery. So the dropping is valid and the condition is void. Similarly, if he sells his pre-emption for money, as we explained. The analogy of our issue is that if he says to a woman with the option of choice, 'Choose for one thousand' or says to his wife 'Choose' for leaving the right of rescission for one thousand, and she chooses, the option falls and the money is not established. The guarantee of a person (kafala bi-nafs) in this is like pre-emption in one narration; in another, the guarantee is not nullified and the money is not obligatory. He said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir: If the consideration is not obligatory, then it should be that his pre-emption is not nullified, as in kafala. The difference is that the right of the pre-emptor has fallen for a consideration in meaning, because the price is delivered to him, while the beneficiary of the guarantee did not consent to the dropping of his right against the guarantor without consideration, and he did not obtain a consideration in meaning because the price was delivered to him originally, so his right of guarantee does not fall. End. The commentator said: The correct view is that both the guarantee and pre-emption fall, and the money is not obligatory. He restricted it by saying 'his pre-emption' because if he settles for taking a share of the house for half the price, it is permissible. If he settles for a room for its share of the price, the settlement is not valid and his pre-emption does not fall because he did not turn away, but the price is unknown, and such ignorance prevents the validity of a sale from the beginning, and taking by pre-emption is a sale. In al-Mabsut: The pre-emptor bargained with the buyer or asked him to assign it to him for that price, and he said yes, so it is a surrender from him.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage enumerates the actions that nullify the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The first is 'ترك المواثبة' (leaving rushing) — meaning failing to promptly claim the right after knowledge and ability. The second is 'التقرير' (affirmation) which refers to accepting or not objecting. The third is settling for a consideration: if the pre-emptor enters into a compromise (sulh) with the buyer in exchange for money, his right is nullified, but he must return the consideration because the right of pre-emption is not a property that can be sold. The text also discusses nuances: if the pre-emptor says 'I drop my pre-emption on condition that you drop yours' etc., the dropping is effective regardless of the condition. The analogy with the option of choice (khiyar al-takhyir) and guarantee (kafala) is made. The correct view according to the commentator is that both shuf'a and kafala are nullified without obligation to pay. Additionally, if the pre-emptor merely asks the buyer to sell to him at the same price, it is considered a surrender of his right. The passage thus clarifies the boundaries of the right of pre-emption, ensuring it is a limited right that must be exercised promptly and cannot be commodified.

Key Terms
المواثبة (al-muwathaba): Rushing or hastening: the act of promptly demanding the right of pre-emption after knowledge.
التقرير (al-taqrir): Affirmation or confirmation: the act of not objecting or affirming the sale, which nullifies the right.
الصلح (al-sulh): Settlement: an agreement to drop the right of pre-emption in exchange for some consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a pre-emptor settles with the buyer for a consideration to drop his right of pre-emption?
A) The pre-emption right remains valid and the consideration is kept.
B) The pre-emption is nullified, but the consideration must be returned.
C) The settlement is void and the right remains.
D) The pre-emption is transferred to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The right of pre-emption is nullified by failing to claim promptly, by acquiescence, or by any settlement for consideration—and the consideration taken in settlement must be returned.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
No Pre-emption for Seller or His Agent
Ruling with proof (talil) denying pre-emption to seller and his agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on pre-emption for sellers and buyers, apply it to agency situations, and explain the underlying causal reasoning (tamlik vs tamalluk).

Core Idea
A seller cannot pre-empt because it would undo his own transfer; a buyer can because it confirms his acquisition.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right to replace a buyer in a sale of real estate. But this right is not absolute: it hinges on whether the claimant's prior actions are consistent with claiming ownership. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that a seller—and anyone acting on his behalf—forfeits pre-emption, while a buyer and his representatives retain it. The reasoning is based on the inherent conflict between selling (transferring ownership) and pre-empting (taking ownership).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text distinguishes between (1) one who sold or for whom sale was made (seller and his agent), and (2) one who bought or for whom purchase was made (buyer and his agent). The ruling is denial of pre-emption for the first and entitlement for the second.
Note that 'for whom sale was made' refers to the principal whose agent sold.
2
Understand the causal principle (talil)
The proof lies in the conflict between tamlik (transfer of ownership by the seller) and tamalluk (taking ownership by pre-emption). Pre-emption would nullify the seller's own act; hence it is prohibited for him. For the buyer, pre-emption is like another purchase and does not conflict.
This is a purely rational proof, not based on a specific text.
3
Apply to agency scenarios
The same rule applies whether the act is done by the principal or his agent. Therefore, an agent who sells loses his own right and also deprives his principal. Conversely, an agent who buys gains the right for both.
Examples given: mudarib (speculator) selling loses right for both; buying grants right to the capital owner.
4
Explore special cases
The commentary discusses the mudarib, ma'dhun slave, fuduli (unauthorized agent), and the seller's inability to claim pre-emption for an adjacent property. The buyer's pre-emption is not invalidated if he competes with other pre-emptors.
In al-Tajrid, a person who sells a house while being its pre-emptor still has pre-emption—this appears contradictory but is reconciled.
5
Synthesize the rule
The overarching principle: any act of sale by the claimant or his representative disqualifies him from pre-empting that property or a related one; any act of purchase qualifies him, as it aligns with the nature of pre-emption.
This rule is consistent across Hanafi texts and considered agreed upon.

قال رحمه الله: (ولا شفعة لمن باع أو بيع له بالوكالة والأصل فيه أن من باع أو بيع ومن اشترى أو اشتري له كان له الشفعة لأن الأخذ بالشفعة في الأول يلزم منه نقض ما تم من جهته وهو بالبيع لأن البيع تمليك والأخذ تملك وبينهما منافاة، وفي الثاني لا يلزم ذلك بل فيه تقريره لأن الأخذ بالشفعة مثل الشراء، ولا فرق بين أن يكون ذلك صدر من الأصيل أو الوكيل حتى لا تكون له الشفعة في الأول ولا لموكله وفي الثاني لهما ذلك، فلو باع المضارب أو العبد المأذون العقار ليس للمولى ولا لرب المال الأخذ بالشفعة، ولو اشترياها كان لرب المال الشفعة لما ذكرنا وكذا للمولى إن كان على العبد دين وإن لم يكن عليه دين فائدة بالأخذ لأنه ملكه والمخير للعقد الذي باشره الفضولي كالموكل لما عرف . وفائدة قوله أن المشتري لا تبطل شفعته إن شارك غيره من الشفعاء إن لم يتقدموا عليه، وإن تقدم هو على من هو بعده من الشفعاء فهي تسلم له عند ترك غيره من الشفعاء والبائع ليس له أن يطلب المبيع بالشفعة في دار أخرى غيرها بلزقها لأنه لما باعها رغب عنها والأخذ رغبة فيها فتنافيا بخلاف المشتري. وفي التجريد ومن باع دارهم وهو شفيعها فله الشفعة اهـ. والظاهر أنه ومن اشترى داراً ولا يخفى أن قوله ولا شفعة لمن باع متكرر مع قوله «وبيع ما يشفع» كما تقدم .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (No pre-emption for one who sold or for whom sale was made by agency). The principle is that one who sold or for whom sale was made, and one who bought or for whom purchase was made, has pre-emption. Because taking by pre-emption in the first necessitates nullifying what was completed from his side, i.e., the sale, since sale is tamlik (transfer of ownership) and taking is tamalluk (taking ownership); there is conflict. In the second, that does not apply; rather, it confirms it because taking by pre-emption is like purchase. There is no difference whether this is done by the principal or the agent, so that he has no pre-emption in the first nor for his principal; in the second, both have it. Thus, if a mudarib or a ma'dhun slave sells real estate, the master or the owner of capital cannot take by pre-emption. If they bought it, the owner of capital has pre-emption for what we mentioned, and likewise the master if the slave has debt; if not, there is benefit in taking because it becomes his property, and the mukhayyar for the contract done by a fuduli is like the principal. The benefit of his statement: the buyer's pre-emption is not invalidated if he shares with other pre-emptors if they do not precede him; if he precedes those after him, the property is awarded to him when others abandon. The buyer cannot claim pre-emption for the sold property in another house adjacent because by selling he has rejected it and taking is desire, so they conflict. The buyer is different. In al-Tajrid: One who sold their house while being its pre-emptor has pre-emption. End. It is apparent that he who bought a house... and it is not hidden that his statement 'no pre-emption for one who sold' overlaps with his statement 'and selling what is pre-empted' as previously.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This lengthy passage deals with the third clause of the matn: no pre-emption for one who sold or for whom sale was made. The principle is clear: a seller cannot claim pre-emption because that would undo his own sale—he willingly transferred ownership, so he cannot then compel transfer to himself. An agent who sells on behalf of another also loses his own right, and the principal loses it as well because the act is attributed to the principal. In contrast, a buyer (or one for whom purchase is made) does have pre-emption because buying is akin to taking ownership, and there is no conflict. The text gives examples: a mudarib (speculator) or a slave with permission to trade – if they sell property, the capital owner or master cannot claim pre-emption; if they buy, those principals can. The commentary also explains that a buyer who is also a pre-emptor does not lose his pre-emption if he shares with others; he can still claim. But a seller cannot claim pre-emption for an adjacent property because by selling he has shown disinterest. The exception from al-Tajrid: if a person sells a house while he is also a pre-emptor of that same house (i.e., he sold his share? unclear), he still has pre-emption. This appears contradictory but is reconciled in the text.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership by the seller.
تملك (tamalluk): Taking ownership by pre-emption.
مضارب (mudarib): Agent in a mudaraba partnership who handles trade.
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): Slave permitted by master to engage in trade.
فضولي (fuduli): Unauthorized agent who contracts without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudarib (speculation partner) sells a property belonging to the partnership, who loses the right of pre-emption?
A) The mudarib only.
B) The owner of capital (rabb al-mal) only.
C) Both the mudarib and the owner of capital.
D) Neither; the right is transferred to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
A seller and his representative (whether principal or agent) forfeit pre-emption due to the inherent conflict between selling and claiming ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
10 min
Who Has and Who Loses Preemption
Explaining the foundational principle of preemption invalidation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a seller (or one for whom property is sold) loses preemption, while a buyer (or one for whom property is bought) retains it, and explain the reasoning.

Core Idea
A seller cannot claim preemption; a buyer retains the right because sale and taking by preemption contradict.

Preemption is a right to acquire sold property, but one who voluntarily sells or ratifies a sale has no standing to later claim the property back via preemption. Conversely, a buyer (even by agent) still holds the right for future preemption claims. This lesson unpacks the core principle from the Hanafi text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
From the text, note that 'man bā‘a' (whoever sells) and 'man ishtarā' (whoever buys) are the two poles. Selling invalidates preemption; buying preserves it.
This includes themselves or for them via agent.
2
Understand the legal rationale
Sale is tamlik (transfer of ownership) while preemption is tamalluk (acquisition). They are contradictory. The seller expressed disinterest; the buyer expressed interest.
The text says: 'فيهما منافاة' (there is contradiction).
3
Apply to agents and principals
An agent's action is attributed to the principal. So if the agent sells, the principal loses preemption; if the agent buys, the principal gains preemption.
Exception: mudārib or ma’dhūn slave's sale does not affect the master's preemption.
4
Note the exception in al-Tajrīd
If a person sells a property while being a preemptor for another property, he still retains preemption for the latter, because the sale was not of the preempted property.
This is a nuanced point: the invalidation applies only to the property sold.

ابن سماعة. ولو قال الشفيع هذه الدار لي فإن أقمت البينة وإلا أخذتها بالشفعة فلا شفعة لأنه ادعى ملكها والشفعة للتملك ويمتنع أن يملك ما هو على ملكه والشفعة حقه فلا يجوز أن يتملك بالعوض ره ابن سماعة عن هذه الخصومة لأنا لو علمنا بالوكالة كان خصماً لأن حقوق العقد تتعلق به فكذا إذا لم تكن معلومة. ولو قال المشتري قبل أن يخاصمه الشفيع اشتريت لفلان وسلم ثم حضر الشفيع فلا خصومه بينه وبين لأن إقراره قبل الخصومة لفلان كما لو كانت الوكالة معلومة، ولو أقر بذلك بعد ما خاصمه الشفيع لم تسقط الخصومة عنه لأنه صار خصماً للشفيع وهو بهذا الإقرار يريد إسقاط حقه فلا يملكه ولو أقام بينة أنه قال قبل شرائه أنه وكيل عند القاضي جاز تسليمه لأن من ملك الأخذ بالشفعة ملك التسليم كما في الأب والوصي ولا يجوز عند غير القاضي عندهما. وقال أبو يوسف : يجوز بناء على أن الوكيل إذا أقر على موكله بالتسليم في غير مجلس الحاكم يقبل لما يأتي في الوكالة للدار شفيعان فوكلا رجلاً فقال سلمت شفعة المشتري صحيح لم
Book of Preemption, Chapter on what invalidates preemption: If the seller stipulates an option for a third party and he ratifies, then he is like the seller. Or if he guarantees the defect [damān al-darak] from the seller. And whoever buys or is bought for has preemption. If it is said to the preemptor that it was sold for a thousand, and he accepted, then he learns it was sold for less, or he was bought for, then there is no preemption for him except that the right of taking is established for him because his purchase there did not imply abandonment. He (the author) said: There is no preemption for one who sold or for whom it was sold by agency. The principle is that whoever sells or is sold for, and whoever buys or is bought for, has preemption. Because taking by preemption in the first entails nullifying what was completed from his side, which is the sale, because sale is transfer of ownership and taking is acquisition, and there is contradiction between them. In the second, that does not follow, rather it is confirmation because taking by preemption is like purchase. There is no difference whether that occurred from the principal or the agent, so that there is no preemption for him in the first nor for his principal; in the second, both have it. So if a mudārib or a ma’dhūn slave sells real estate, neither the master nor the rabb al-māl has the right to take by preemption; if they buy it, then the rabb al-māl has preemption as we mentioned, likewise the master if the slave has debt, and if there is no debt, then benefit is in taking because he owns it. The one who chooses the contract performed by a fudūlī is like the principal. The benefit of his statement: the buyer's preemption is not invalidated if he shares with other preemptors as long as they do not precede him; if he precedes those after him, it is given to him when others leave. The seller does not have the right to demand the sold property by preemption in another house adjacent, because when he sold it he expressed disinterest, and taking is interest, so they contradict, unlike the buyer. And in al-Tajrīd: whoever sold their house while being a preemptor has preemption. It is apparent that whoever buys a house... and it is not hidden that his statement 'no preemption for one who sold' is repeated with 'and what is sold for preemption' as previously. He said: If the seller stipulates an option for a third party and he ratifies, he is like the seller. If the buyer did that and ratified, he is like the buyer. We have explained it. He said: Or he guaranteed the defect from the seller, meaning if the preemptor guarantees the defect from the seller, then there is no preemption for him because the completion of the sale was from his side, so he cannot nullify what was completed from his side. We have explained it. He said: Whoever buys or is bought for has preemption, and we have explained its face previously. In Fatāwā al-Fadlī: The agent to buy a house, if he takes possession of the house while it is in his hand, the preemptor demands from him and takes it; if he delivered the house to the principal, the preemptor demands from the principal and takes from him, not from the agent. In Jāmi‘ al-Fatāwā: The agent bought, then the preemptor came, he takes it from the agent and does not pay attention to the presence of the principal. If he is an agent to sell and sells, then the preemptor comes, he takes it from the agent. It is related from Abu Yusuf that the agent to buy does not own the taking. In al-Jāmi‘: A house has two preemptors; the buyer said to one of them: I bought the house for you. If he verifies him, his right is not invalidated even if he later confessed that he did not command him, because we are certain of the confirmation of preemption for him by the purchase, whether the buyer bought the house for himself or for the one he confessed to by his command, because whoever buys or is bought for has preemption, and nothing that would invalidate it occurred from him, because his ownership of the house by purchase is a demand from him for preemption and an increase, because whoever demands preemption acquires the house by preemption in the second demand. When he owns it, al-Bahr al-Rā’iq j 8/174, Book of Preemption, Chapter on what invalidates preemption: what is his property; he mentioned it, it came correctly from him at the moment; it takes the place of demand and increase. If the buyer said: This house was entirely yours, not mine nor the seller's, or said: I had bought it before, or the seller said: I gifted it to you, and he verified him, his preemption is invalidated. If he did not verify him, the preemptor has the right to take, so he may take it all by preemption because the purchase is established in apparent reality; preemption is established for the two preemptors after it was confirmed for them in apparent reality, so the right of the one who confirmed is invalidated by his confirmation, and the right of the one who denied is not invalidated because they both confirm against him. In al-Nawādir: If the preemptor confessed before judgment for preemption that this house belongs to such absent person and that he did not order him to sell, and the buyer said it is for the seller, his preemption is not invalidated. Similarly, if the seller said: The owner authorized me to sell it, and the preemptor said: The owner did not authorize him, then he has preemption, because the preemptor's statement is not believed against the two transacting parties, so the sold item is ruled valid in their regard, so it is permissible for the preemptor to claim his rights. Similarly, if a man claimed this house and this preemptor testified for him but was not just, then the possessor sold it, the preemptor may take it by preemption; mentioned by Ibn Samā‘ah. If the preemptor said: This house is mine, if I produce evidence, otherwise I take it by preemption, then there is no preemption because he claimed ownership, and preemption is for acquisition, and it is impossible to acquire what is already his property, and preemption is his right, so he is not permitted to acquire it with compensation; related by Ibn Samā‘ah from Abu Yusuf. In the previous issues, he acknowledged that the thing is under the ownership of another, so it is permissible for him to acquire it with compensation. This is if it is known that he is an agent to buy, we have given its rule. If that is not known except by his statement and the preemptor denies the agency, then he is a disputant, and there is no benefit in this dispute because if we knew the agency, he would be a disputant because the rights of the contract attach to him, so likewise if it is not known. If the buyer said before the preemptor disputes him: I bought it for so-and-so and delivered, then the preemptor came, there is no dispute between him and the buyer because his admission before the dispute is for so-and-so, as if the agency was known. If he admitted that after the preemptor disputed him, the dispute is not dropped from him because he became a disputant for the preemptor, and with this admission he wants to drop his right, so he does not have the power to do so. If he produces evidence that he said before his purchase that he is an agent for so-and-so, his evidence is not accepted because he repels the dispute from himself with this evidence. It is related from Muhammad that his evidence is accepted to repel the dispute until the one he admitted for appears. The agent to demand preemption is a disputant because taking by preemption includes purchase and dispute, and agency for both is permissible except according to Abu Hanifa, because it is not valid except with the consent of the opponent. According to the two, it is valid without the consent of the opponent. If the agent of the preemptor demanded, and the buyer said: The preemptor has left (surrendered), his statement is not accepted. Similarly, if he wanted him to swear that he is negligent in demanding preemption, but he is ordered to deliver the house to the agent, then the principal is followed and asked to take an oath. This is like the agent for receiving a debt when the debtor claims discharge from the principal; he is ordered to pay the debt to the agent, then the principal is followed and asked to take an oath. If the agent surrendered the preemption or confessed to surrender before the judge, his surrender is valid because whoever owns the taking of preemption owns the surrender, as in the case of father and executor. However, it is not valid outside the court according to the two. Abu Yusuf said: It is valid based on the principle that if the agent confesses to surrender on behalf of his principal outside the court, it is accepted, as will come in the chapter of agency. For a house with two preemptors, they appointed a man, and he said: I surrendered the preemption. The buyer's [statement is] correct? Not complete.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion (sharh) on the chapter 'What Invalidates Preemption' from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The author, likely Ibn Nujaym, explains several scenarios. The first ruling: if the seller stipulates an option for a third party and that party ratifies the sale, the seller is treated as if he himself ratified; thus preemption is invalid for him because he was instrumental in completing the sale. Similarly, if the preemptor guarantees the seller against defects (damān al-darak), his preemption is invalid because he is the reason the sale was finalized. The central principle: a person who sells property or for whom property is sold (e.g., via an agent) cannot claim preemption because that would contradict his own action of selling (which expresses disinterest). Conversely, a person who buys property or for whom property is bought (e.g., by an agent) retains preemption because buying is akin to seeking ownership. This applies whether the person acts directly or through an agent. For example, if a mudārib or a ma’dhūn slave sells real estate, neither the master nor the capital owner can claim preemption; if they buy it, they have preemption. The text then discusses agents in detail: if an agent to buy takes possession, the preemptor demands from the agent; if delivered to the principal, from the principal. There is a known dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that an agent to buy does not own the right to take preemption (i.e., cannot be a plaintiff), while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that he does. The passage also covers acknowledgments: if the buyer tells a preemptor 'I bought this for you' and the preemptor verifies it, his preemption is not invalidated (he still has it) because his right was established by the purchase. However, if he verifies a claim that the house was always his, or that it was gifted to him, then his preemption is invalidated because he claims ownership. The text notes that the preemptor's statement against the buyer and seller regarding ownership is not believed; the sale is presumed valid. It also discusses the timing of admissions: an admission before dispute is effective, after dispute it is not. The passage ends with a discussion on agents for demanding preemption and the validity of surrender.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf‘a): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to acquire the sold share of a property by paying the price to the buyer, based on the principle of preventing harm.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership (as in sale).
التملك (al-tamalluk): Acquisition of ownership (as in preemption).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, if a person sells his house through an agent, can he later claim preemption on that same house if another co-owner sells?
A) Yes, because his agent sold, not him directly.
B) No, because the agent's sale is attributed to him, and selling invalidates his preemption.
C) Yes, because preemption is a personal right that cannot be lost.
D) No, but only if the agent was not authorized.

Key Takeaway
Selling (even by agent) invalidates preemption for that property; buying (even by agent) preserves it.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Agency and Preemption: The Agent's Role and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining agency rules and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf in preemption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf regarding the agent's capacity to take and surrender preemption, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
An agent to buy can take preemption; but surrendering preemption outside court is invalid according to the majority.

Preemption involves both acquisition and litigation, and agency for both is generally valid. However, disagreements arose within the Hanafi school regarding whether an agent appointed to buy can personally claim preemption, and whether an agent's surrender of preemption outside court is binding. This lesson examines the details of agency in preemption and the resolution of the differences.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule: agent's action attributed to principal
Recall from lesson 1 that an agent's sale or purchase is attributed to the principal for preemption purposes. But here we ask: Can the agent himself act as a preemptor (e.g., when the agent buys with his own money)?
2
Identify the first dispute: agent to buy taking preemption
Abu Yusuf holds that an agent to buy does not own the right to take preemption (i.e., cannot be a plaintiff in court). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that he can.
The text says: 'وعن أبي يوسف: الوكيل بالشراء لا يملك الأخذ'.
3
Analyze the second dispute: agent's surrender outside court
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, an agent's surrender of preemption is valid only in front of a judge. Abu Yusuf allows it outside court based on the principle that an agent's confession is accepted.
The text: 'وقال أبو يوسف : يجوز بناء على أن الوكيل إذا أقر على موكله بالتسليم في غير مجلس الحاكم يقبل'.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferred position)
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon for fatwa because it safeguards the principal's interest and follows the principle that agency for litigation requires judicial oversight.
Later Hanafi authorities affirm this tarjih.
5
Apply the rulings to practical scenarios
If an agent to buy takes possession of a house, the preemptor can demand from the agent. If the agent surrenders outside court, it is not binding on the principal (majority view).
The text from Fatāwā al-Fadlī gives similar scenarios.

Book of Preemption, Chapter on what invalidates preemption: If the seller stipulates an option for a third party and he ratifies, then he is like the seller. Or if he guarantees the defect [damān al-darak] from the seller. And whoever buys or is bought for has preemption. If it is said to the preemptor that it was sold for a thousand, and he accepted, then he learns it was sold for less, or he was bought for, then there is no preemption for him except that the right of taking is established for him because his purchase there did not imply abandonment. He (the author) said: There is no preemption for one who sold or for whom it was sold by agency. The principle is that whoever sells or is sold for, and whoever buys or is bought for, has preemption. Because taking by preemption in the first entails nullifying what was completed from his side, which is the sale, because sale is transfer of ownership and taking is acquisition, and there is contradiction between them. In the second, that does not follow, rather it is confirmation because taking by preemption is like purchase. There is no difference whether that occurred from the principal or the agent, so that there is no preemption for him in the first nor for his principal; in the second, both have it. So if a mudārib or a ma’dhūn slave sells real estate, neither the master nor the rabb al-māl has the right to take by preemption; if they buy it, then the rabb al-māl has preemption as we mentioned, likewise the master if the slave has debt, and if there is no debt, then benefit is in taking because he owns it. The one who chooses the contract performed by a fudūlī is like the principal. The benefit of his statement: the buyer's preemption is not invalidated if he shares with other preemptors as long as they do not precede him; if he precedes those after him, it is given to him when others leave. The seller does not have the right to demand the sold property by preemption in another house adjacent, because when he sold it he expressed disinterest, and taking is interest, so they contradict, unlike the buyer. And in al-Tajrīd: whoever sold their house while being a preemptor has preemption. It is apparent that whoever buys a house... and it is not hidden that his statement 'no preemption for one who sold' is repeated with 'and what is sold for preemption' as previously. He said: If the seller stipulates an option for a third party and he ratifies, he is like the seller. If the buyer did that and ratified, he is like the buyer. We have explained it. He said: Or he guaranteed the defect from the seller, meaning if the preemptor guarantees the defect from the seller, then there is no preemption for him because the completion of the sale was from his side, so he cannot nullify what was completed from his side. We have explained it. He said: Whoever buys or is bought for has preemption, and we have explained its face previously. In Fatāwā al-Fadlī: The agent to buy a house, if he takes possession of the house while it is in his hand, the preemptor demands from him and takes it; if he delivered the house to the principal, the preemptor demands from the principal and takes from him, not from the agent. In Jāmi‘ al-Fatāwā: The agent bought, then the preemptor came, he takes it from the agent and does not pay attention to the presence of the principal. If he is an agent to sell and sells, then the preemptor comes, he takes it from the agent. It is related from Abu Yusuf that the agent to buy does not own the taking. In al-Jāmi‘: A house has two preemptors; the buyer said to one of them: I bought the house for you. If he verifies him, his right is not invalidated even if he later confessed that he did not command him, because we are certain of the confirmation of preemption for him by the purchase, whether the buyer bought the house for himself or for the one he confessed to by his command, because whoever buys or is bought for has preemption, and nothing that would invalidate it occurred from him, because his ownership of the house by purchase is a demand from him for preemption and an increase, because whoever demands preemption acquires the house by preemption in the second demand. When he owns it, al-Bahr al-Rā’iq j 8/174, Book of Preemption, Chapter on what invalidates preemption: what is his property; he mentioned it, it came correctly from him at the moment; it takes the place of demand and increase. If the buyer said: This house was entirely yours, not mine nor the seller's, or said: I had bought it before, or the seller said: I gifted it to you, and he verified him, his preemption is invalidated. If he did not verify him, the preemptor has the right to take, so he may take it all by preemption because the purchase is established in apparent reality; preemption is established for the two preemptors after it was confirmed for them in apparent reality, so the right of the one who confirmed is invalidated by his confirmation, and the right of the one who denied is not invalidated because they both confirm against him. In al-Nawādir: If the preemptor confessed before judgment for preemption that this house belongs to such absent person and that he did not order him to sell, and the buyer said it is for the seller, his preemption is not invalidated. Similarly, if the seller said: The owner authorized me to sell it, and the preemptor said: The owner did not authorize him, then he has preemption, because the preemptor's statement is not believed against the two transacting parties, so the sold item is ruled valid in their regard, so it is permissible for the preemptor to claim his rights. Similarly, if a man claimed this house and this preemptor testified for him but was not just, then the possessor sold it, the preemptor may take it by preemption; mentioned by Ibn Samā‘ah. If the preemptor said: This house is mine, if I produce evidence, otherwise I take it by preemption, then there is no preemption because he claimed ownership, and preemption is for acquisition, and it is impossible to acquire what is already his property, and preemption is his right, so he is not permitted to acquire it with compensation; related by Ibn Samā‘ah from Abu Yusuf. In the previous issues, he acknowledged that the thing is under the ownership of another, so it is permissible for him to acquire it with compensation. This is if it is known that he is an agent to buy, we have given its rule. If that is not known except by his statement and the preemptor denies the agency, then he is a disputant, and there is no benefit in this dispute because if we knew the agency, he would be a disputant because the rights of the contract attach to him, so likewise if it is not known. If the buyer said before the preemptor disputes him: I bought it for so-and-so and delivered, then the preemptor came, there is no dispute between him and the buyer because his admission before the dispute is for so-and-so, as if the agency was known. If he admitted that after the preemptor disputed him, the dispute is not dropped from him because he became a disputant for the preemptor, and with this admission he wants to drop his right, so he does not have the power to do so. If he produces evidence that he said before his purchase that he is an agent for so-and-so, his evidence is not accepted because he repels the dispute from himself with this evidence. It is related from Muhammad that his evidence is accepted to repel the dispute until the one he admitted for appears. The agent to demand preemption is a disputant because taking by preemption includes purchase and dispute, and agency for both is permissible except according to Abu Hanifa, because it is not valid except with the consent of the opponent. According to the two, it is valid without the consent of the opponent. If the agent of the preemptor demanded, and the buyer said: The preemptor has left (surrendered), his statement is not accepted. Similarly, if he wanted him to swear that he is negligent in demanding preemption, but he is ordered to deliver the house to the agent, then the principal is followed and asked to take an oath. This is like the agent for receiving a debt when the debtor claims discharge from the principal; he is ordered to pay the debt to the agent, then the principal is followed and asked to take an oath. If the agent surrendered the preemption or confessed to surrender before the judge, his surrender is valid because whoever owns the taking of preemption owns the surrender, as in the case of father and executor. However, it is not valid outside the court according to the two. Abu Yusuf said: It is valid based on the principle that if the agent confesses to surrender on behalf of his principal outside the court, it is accepted, as will come in the chapter of agency. For a house with two preemptors, they appointed a man, and he said: I surrendered the preemption. The buyer's [statement is] correct? Not complete.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion (sharh) on the chapter 'What Invalidates Preemption' from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The author, likely Ibn Nujaym, explains several scenarios. The first ruling: if the seller stipulates an option for a third party and that party ratifies the sale, the seller is treated as if he himself ratified; thus preemption is invalid for him because he was instrumental in completing the sale. Similarly, if the preemptor guarantees the seller against defects (damān al-darak), his preemption is invalid because he is the reason the sale was finalized. The central principle: a person who sells property or for whom property is sold (e.g., via an agent) cannot claim preemption because that would contradict his own action of selling (which expresses disinterest). Conversely, a person who buys property or for whom property is bought (e.g., by an agent) retains preemption because buying is akin to seeking ownership. This applies whether the person acts directly or through an agent. For example, if a mudārib or a ma’dhūn slave sells real estate, neither the master nor the capital owner can claim preemption; if they buy it, they have preemption. The text then discusses agents in detail: if an agent to buy takes possession, the preemptor demands from the agent; if delivered to the principal, from the principal. There is a known dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that an agent to buy does not own the right to take preemption (i.e., cannot be a plaintiff), while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that he does. The passage also covers acknowledgments: if the buyer tells a preemptor 'I bought this for you' and the preemptor verifies it, his preemption is not invalidated (he still has it) because his right was established by the purchase. However, if he verifies a claim that the house was always his, or that it was gifted to him, then his preemption is invalidated because he claims ownership. The text notes that the preemptor's statement against the buyer and seller regarding ownership is not believed; the sale is presumed valid. It also discusses the timing of admissions: an admission before dispute is effective, after dispute it is not. The passage ends with a discussion on agents for demanding preemption and the validity of surrender.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالشراء (al-wakīl bi al-shirā'): An agent appointed to purchase a specific property.
التسليم (al-taslīm): Here: surrender of preemption right, i.e., relinquishing the right to take the property.
الخصومة (al-khusūma): Litigation or dispute in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muftā bihi in the Hanafi school, when can an agent for the preemptor validly surrender preemption?
A) Anytime and anywhere.
B) Only in front of a judge (court).
C) Only if the principal is present.
D) Only if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
The agent to buy can take preemption (majority), but his surrender of preemption is only valid in court to protect the principal's rights.

Effect of the Pre-emptor's Admission on His Right to Pre-emption
Rulings on how the shafi'i's statements about ownership affect his right to pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios where a shafi'i's statement nullifies his pre-emption right and apply them to new cases.

Core Idea
Admitting ownership or confirming the property is not for sale nullifies pre-emption; merely denying the sale does not.

In the chapter on pre-emption (shuf'a), one must know what actions or words of the pre-emptor (shafi'i) invalidate his right. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains several scenarios: when the shafi'i confirms the buyer's claim that the property already belongs to him, when he claims ownership himself, and when his statements are merely about the validity of the sale. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for a shafi'i to protect his right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shafi'i's statement
Read the scenario and determine what exactly the shafi'i said. Is it a confirmation of the buyer's statement, a claim of ownership, or a denial of the seller's authority?
2
Apply the default rule for confirmation
If the shafi'i confirms the buyer's statement that the property is already his (or was gifted to him), his pre-emption is nullified. If he denies it, his right remains.
The text says: 'if he confirms him, his pre-emption is nullified'.
3
Distinguish a claim of ownership from a statement about validity
If the shafi'i claims 'this house is mine', his pre-emption is void because he is asserting ownership of what he seeks to acquire by pre-emption. Statements that merely question the seller's authority (e.g., 'the owner did not authorize the sale') do not nullify.
The passage explicitly says: 'if the shafi'i says 'this house is mine'... no pre-emption.'
4
Recognize the exception from al-Nawadir
In a case from al-Nawadir, if the shafi'i acknowledges that the property belongs to an absentee (and does not claim it for himself) and the buyer says it belongs to the seller, the shafi'i's pre-emption is not nullified. His statement is not binding on the parties.
The ruling from al-Nawadir: 'his pre-emption is not nullified'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال المشتري هذه الدار كلها كانت لك ولم تكن لي ولا للبائع أو قال كنت اشتريتها قبل أو قال البائع وهبها لك فصدقه بطلت شفعته، ولو لم يصدقه على ذلك للشفيع الأخذ فله أن يأخذ كلها بالشفعة لأن الشراء قد . حيث الظاهر وجبت الشفعة للشفيعين بعد ما ثبت لهما من حيث الظاهر فبطل حق المصدق لتصديقه ولم يبطل حق المكذب لأنهما يصدقان عليه. وفي النوادر : ولو أقر الشفيع قبل القضاء له بالشفعة أن هذه الدار لفلان الغائب وأنه لم يأمره بالبيع وقال المشتري بل هو للبائع لم تبطل شفعته، وكذلك لو قال البائع وكلني صاحبها بالبيع وقال الشفيع لم يأمره صاحبها بالبيع فله الشفعة فله الشفعة لأن قول الشفيع لا يصدق في حق المتبايعين فكان المبيع محكوماً بصحته في حقهما فجاز للشفيع أن يطالب بحقوقه، وكذلك لو ادعى هذه الدار رجل فشهد له هذا الشفيع فلم يعدل ثم باعها ذو اليد فللشفيع أن يأخذها بالشفعة؛ ذكره ابن سماعة. ولو قال الشفيع هذه الدار لي فإن أقمت البينة وإلا أخذتها بالشفعة فلا شفعة لأنه ادعى ملكها والشفعة للتملك ويمتنع أن يملك ما هو على ملكه والشفعة حقه فلا يجوز أن يتملك بالعوض.
And if the buyer says, 'This entire house belongs to you, and it was not mine nor the seller's,' or he says, 'I bought it before,' or the seller says, 'He gave it to you as a gift,' and he (the shafi'i) confirms him, his pre-emption is nullified. If he does not confirm him, the shafi'i may take; so he may take the entire house by pre-emption because the purchase is established apparently. Pre-emption is established for the two shafi'is after it was established for them apparently, so the right of the one who confirmed (the statement) is nullified because of his confirmation, and the right of the one who denied is not nullified because they both confirm (the purchase) against him. And in al-Nawadir: If the shafi'i acknowledges before judgment that this house belongs to such-and-such absentee and that he did not authorize him to sell, and the buyer says, 'Rather it is for the seller,' his pre-emption is not nullified. Similarly, if the seller says, 'Its owner authorized me to sell,' and the shafi'i says, 'Its owner did not authorize him to sell,' then he has pre-emption, because the shafi'i's statement is not accepted against the two parties to the sale, so the sold item is ruled valid in their regard, so it is permissible for the shafi'i to claim his rights. Similarly, if a man claims this house and this shafi'i testifies for him but his testimony is not accepted, then the possessor sells it, the shafi'i may take it by pre-emption; mentioned by Ibn Suma'a. And if the shafi'i says, 'This house is mine; if I bring evidence, otherwise I will take it by pre-emption,' then he has no pre-emption because he claimed ownership of it, and pre-emption is for acquiring ownership, and it is impossible to own what is already his property, and pre-emption is his right, so it is not permissible to acquire it by compensation.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings from the chapter on what nullifies pre-emption. The first ruling concerns a scenario where the buyer informs the shafi'i that the property already belongs to him (the shafi'i) or has been gifted to him, and the shafi'i confirms this. In such a case, the shafi'i's right to pre-emption is nullified because he has admitted that the property is already his, so he cannot seek to acquire what he already owns. However, if the shafi'i denies the buyer's statement, his right to pre-emption remains intact, as the apparent ownership still lies with the buyer. The second ruling, narrated from al-Nawadir, covers a situation where the shafi'i acknowledges before the court that the property belongs to an absent person and that he did not authorize the sale, yet the buyer claims it belongs to the seller. In this case, the shafi'i's pre-emption is not nullified because his statement is not binding against the contracting parties; the sale remains valid between them, so the shafi'i can still claim his right. The same applies if the seller claims to be an agent but the shafi'i denies it. The third ruling is when the shafi'i himself asserts ownership of the property, saying 'this house is mine' and then alternatively seeks pre-emption. This directly nullifies his pre-emption right because he claims the property is already his, and pre-emption is a means to acquire ownership—it cannot be used to acquire what one already owns. The Hanafi school emphasizes that pre-emption is a right to purchase, and if the shafi'i's own words contradict that, his right is forfeited.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of a pre-emptor to take property sold to another by force of law upon payment of the price.
شفيع (shafi'i): The person entitled to pre-emption (the pre-emptor).
النوادر (al-nawadir): A collection of rare or non-standard transmissions in the Hanafi school, often opposing Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the shafi'i says 'this house is mine' and then demands pre-emption?
A) His pre-emption is valid because he is claiming ownership.
B) His pre-emption is nullified because he claimed ownership of the property.
C) His pre-emption is postponed until he proves ownership.
D) He must choose between claiming ownership and pre-emption.

Key Takeaway
A shafi'i who admits prior ownership or claims ownership of the property nullifies his right to pre-emption; mere denial or acknowledgment of another's ownership does not.
