Shuf'a with Cubit Exception and Hiyal to Prevent Shuf'a
Rulings on shuf'a with cubit exception and hiyal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that shuf'a requires direct adjacency (thus a retained cubit strip prevents it) and identify the permissibility conditions for hiyal to prevent shuf'a before obligation, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on makruh.

Core Idea
Shuf'a requires direct adjacency; a retained cubit gap eliminates it. Hiyal to prevent shuf'a before it vests are generally permissible.

This lesson examines two interconnected issues in shuf'a (pre-emption). First, the rule that selling a house except for a cubit strip along the neighbour's boundary severs adjacency and thus nullifies the neighbour's shuf'a right. Second, the use of legal stratagems (hiyal) to prevent or nullify shuf'a. The Hanafi school distinguishes between hiyal that nullify shuf'a after it has vested (generally makruh) and those that prevent it from vesting in the first place (generally permissible, with some disagreement). You will learn the rationale behind these rulings and the points of difference within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule on the cubit exception
Read the opening phrase: 'إذا باع الدار إلا مقدار ذراع في طول الحد الذي يلي الشفيع فلا شفعة له'. The seller retains a strip of land along the neighbour's boundary; thus the sold property is not directly adjacent, and the neighbour's shuf'a is lost.
The key condition for neighbour's shuf'a is shared boundary (juwar). Any gap, even a small strip retained by the seller, breaks adjacency.
2
Distinguish two types of hiyal in shuf'a
According to al-Tatarkhaniya, hiyal are of two types: (1) those that nullify shuf'a after it has become obligatory (e.g., the buyer says 'I sell it to you' and the shafi' agrees – this is a new sale that cancels the original shuf'a); (2) those that prevent shuf'a from ever becoming obligatory (e.g., structuring the sale so that no perfect right arises).
This classification is crucial because the ruling (makruh or not) differs based on timing.
3
Analyse the dispute on hiyal after obligation
The text reports a consensus (from Shaykh al-Islam) that nullifying shuf'a after vesting is makruh. However, Shams al-A'imma says it is not makruh if the buyer does not intend harm. The preferred view (al-Yanabi') is that it is makruh after the sale. This shows that even for post-vesting hiyal, there is nuance.
Intention matters: if the buyer's aim is merely to avoid the shuf'a claim without harming the shafi', some scholars allow it.
4
Understand the dispute on hiyal before obligation
For hiyal that prevent shuf'a from vesting, the mashayikh differed: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is makruh; others say it is not makruh without dispute. The preferred view (al-mukhtar from al-Khulasa) is that it is permissible whether the shafi' is just or sinful. If the shafi' is harmful, it is definitely not makruh.
The text resolves that pre-obligation hiyal are generally allowed, especially when the shafi' is oppressive.
5
Apply the hila to a concrete scenario
The matn gives an example: to dissuade the neighbour from taking shuf'a, the buyer purchases the first share (adjacent) for almost the entire price and the second share for token. The neighbour would have to pay a high price for the first share via shuf'a, making it unattractive. This is a permissible stratagem before the shuf'a right vests.
The hila exploits the rule that shuf'a is taken at the sale price; inflating the price for the adjacent portion legally deters the shafi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذراعاً في جانب الشفيع فلا شفعة له يعني إذا باع الدار إلا مقدار ذراع في طول الحد الذي يلي الشفيع فلا شفعة له لأن الاستحقاق بالجوار ولم يوجد الاتصال بالمبيع، وكذا لو وهب للمشتري لعدم الاتصال وهو حيلة وفي التتارخانية : الحيلة في هذا الباب نوعان: نوع لإسقاطه بعد الوجوب وذلك بأن يقول للشفيع أنا أبيعها منك فقال الشفيع نعم فتبطل شفعته وهو مكروه بالإجماع ؛ كذا ذكره شيخ الإسلام وذكره شمس الأئمة أنه لا يكره إذا لم يقصد المشتري الإضرار بالشفيع وفي الينابيع : قيل الاختلاف قبل المبيع، أما بعده فمكروه بالإجماع وهو الأصح. وفي العتابية ونوع منه يمنع وجوبه وقد اختلف المشايخ قالوا على قول أبي يوسف وعلى قول محمد مكروه. وفي الذخيرة ومنهم من قال في الشفعة لا تكره الحيلة لمنع وجوبها بلا خلاف. وفي الخلاصة : الحيلة لإبطال الشفعة إن كان قبل الوجوب لا بأس به، سواء كان الشفيع عدلاً أو فاسقاً فهو المختار . وفي فتاوى الفضلي عن أبي بكر بن سعيد فقال : الحيلة بعد المبيع مكروهة في الأحوال كلها وقبل البيع إن كان الجار فاسقاً يتأذى به فلا يكره، وقيل يكره في جميع الأحوال ا هـ . رحمه الله : (وإن ابتاع منهما سهماً بثمن ثم ابتاع بقيمتها فالشفعة للجار في السهم الأول (فقط لأن الشفيع جار في السهم الأول والمشتري شريك في السهم الثاني وهو مقدم على الجار، ولو أراد الحيلة يشتري السهم الأول بجميع الثمن إلا درهماً والسهم الثاني بدرهم فلا يرغب الجار في أخذه لكثرة الثمن، وكذا المسألة الأولى ما يأتي مثل هذه الحيلة بأن يبيع ما يلي الجار بجميع الثمن إلا درهماً ثم يشتري الباقي بدرهم فإن أخذه بالشفعة أخذ قدر الذراع بجميع الثمن وليس له أن يأخذ الباقي بدرهم، فإن أخذه بالشفعة أخذ قدر الذراع بجميع الثمن وليس له أن يأخذ الباقي لأنه ليس بجار فأيهما خاف أن لا يوفي صاحبه شرط الخيار لنفسه، وإن خافا شرط كل منهما في قال
A cubit on the side of the shafi': so no shuf'a for him. Meaning, if he sells the house except for the measure of a cubit in length along the boundary adjacent to the shafi', then there is no shuf'a for him, because the entitlement is through adjacency, and connection with the sold property does not exist. Similarly, if he gives it as a gift to the buyer, due to lack of connection, and it is a stratagem (hila). And in al-Tatarkhaniya: The stratagem in this chapter is of two types: a type to nullify it after it becomes obligatory, and that is by saying to the shafi', 'I will sell it to you,' and the shafi' says, 'Yes,' so his shuf'a is invalidated, and it is makruh by consensus. Thus mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam, and Shams al-A'imma mentioned that it is not makruh if the buyer does not intend harm to the shafi'. And in al-Yanabi': It is said the difference before the sale, but after it is makruh by consensus, and that is the most correct. And in al-'Itabiya: And a type of it prevents its obligation, and the mashayikh differed: they said according to Abu Yusuf and according to Muhammad it is makruh. And in al-Dhakhira: And among them is who said in shuf'a, the stratagem is not disliked for preventing its obligation, without dispute. And in al-Khulasa: The stratagem to nullify shuf'a, if it is before obligation, there is no harm in it, whether the shafi' is just or sinful, that is the chosen view. And in Fatawa al-Fadli from Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id, he said: The stratagem after the sale is makruh in all circumstances, and before the sale if the neighbor is sinful, one is harmed by him, then it is not makruh; and it is said it is makruh in all circumstances. End quote. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he purchases from them a share at a price, then purchases the rest at its value, then shuf'a is for the neighbor in the first share only, because the shafi' is a neighbor in the first share, and the buyer is a partner in the second share, and he is preferred over the neighbor. And if he wants a stratagem, he buys the first share for the entire price except one dirham, and the second share for one dirham, so the neighbor will not desire to take it due to the high price. And similarly the first issue: such a stratagem is that he sells what is adjacent to the neighbor for the entire price except one dirham, then buys the remainder for one dirham. If he takes it by shuf'a, he takes the amount of the cubit with the entire price, and he may not take the remainder for one dirham. If he takes it by shuf'a, he takes the amount of the cubit with the entire price, and he may not take the remainder because it is not adjacent. So whichever of them fears that his companion will not fulfill the condition of option for himself, and if they both fear, each of them stipulates... in... said...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: the cubit exception and the permissibility of hiyal (legal stratagems) to prevent shuf'a. First, the matn: if the seller sells a house except for a cubit (about 0.5 meter) of land along the side adjacent to the shafi' neighbor, then the neighbor has no shuf'a. The reason is that shuf'a is based on adjacency (juwar), and if a small strip is retained by the seller, the sold property is not directly adjacent to the shafi'i's property; there is a gap. The same applies if the seller gives that strip as a gift to the buyer—it severs adjacency. This is itself a hila to avoid shuf'a. Then the text from al-Tatarkhaniya discusses hiyal in shuf'a: there are two types. The first type nullifies shuf'a after it has become obligatory (after the sale). An example is the buyer saying to the shafi', 'I will sell it to you,' and the shafi' agrees; that agreement constitutes a new sale, and the shafi' loses his original shuf'a. This is considered makruh (disliked) by consensus according to Shaykh al-Islam, but Shams al-A'imma says it is not makruh if the buyer does not intend harm. The strongest view is that it is makruh if done after the sale. The second type prevents shuf'a from becoming obligatory in the first place. Here the mashayikh differed: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it makruh, but others allow it without dispute. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) from al-Khulasa is that hiyal to prevent shuf'a before it vests are permissible whether the shafi' is just or sinful. Also, if the neighbor is sinful and causes harm, it is not makruh to use a hila. Finally, the matn concludes with a case: if a person buys a share of a property from a co-owner (making him a partner), then later buys the remaining share at its full value, the neighbor (not the partner) gets shuf'a only in the first share because the buyer is a partner in the second share and partners take precedence over neighbors. A hila is then described to dissuade the neighbor: the buyer pays nearly all the price for the first share and only a token for the second, so the neighbor would have to pay a high price to get shuf'a on the first share and would likely refuse. The text ends abruptly with a discussion of options and conditions.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-ḥīla): A legal stratagem used to achieve a lawful result, in this context to prevent or nullify shuf'a.
الجوار (al-jiwār): Adjacency or neighborly relation that can give rise to shuf'a rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on using a hila to prevent shuf'a from becoming obligatory before the sale is completed?
A) It is makruh by consensus.
B) It is permissible without any dislike.
C) It is haram if the shafi' is just.
D) It is only allowed if the buyer intends harm.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a requires direct adjacency; a retained cubit strip nullifies it, and hiyal to prevent shuf'a before it vests are generally permissible, especially when the neighbour is harmful.

Pre-emption Price and Stratagems to Avoid It
Explains that pre-emption is based on price, not substitute, and presents stratagems to avoid it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that pre-emption is based on the price (not the substitute) and explain the legal stratagems (hiyal) that nullify pre-emption, including the intra-Hanafi difference on the absent proxy.

Core Idea
Pre-emption follows the fixed price, not the payment medium; stratagems can legally void it.

A buyer pays for a property with a cloth instead of cash. The pre-emptor must pay the price, not the cloth. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raʾiq explains why and then unveils a series of stratagems that cleverly leverage separate contracts to eliminate the right of pre-emption. Understanding these stratagems is essential for both protecting one's rights and avoiding unfair surprises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling and Its Basis
Determine what the text states: pre-emption is by the price (thaman) not by the substitute (thawb). Then note the rational proof: the cloth is a separate transaction, so it does not change the original price.
The proof is dalīl ʿaqlī; no Quran or hadith is cited.
2
Trace the Reasoning Behind the Stratagems
Examine each stratagem: paying overvalue in dirhams, paying in dinars (sarf transaction), combining dirhams and dinars. Understand why they work: they create independent contracts whose nullity does not affect the original sale.
The danger is harming the seller if the property is reclaimed – the text addresses this.
3
Notice the Conditions and Scope
Identify that the first stratagem is specific to adjacency (jār), while others apply to both neighbour and partner. Also note that minors and the insane are treated as capable adults under certain conditions.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Find the difference between Imām Muḥammad and Abū Yūsuf regarding the absent proxy: Muḥammad says the principal is not a defendant, Abū Yūsuf says he is. Understand the practical effect on who the pre-emptor sues.
This is a legitimate ikhtilāf within the school.
5
Assess the Tarjīḥ and Muftā Bihī
The text does not give a clear preference, but the extensive listing of stratagems implies their permissibility. Later Hanafi works generally accept these stratagems as valid (ṣaḥīḥ).
Other schools may reject them; Hanafis allow them to avoid harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثلاً قال رحمه الله : (وإن ابتاعها بثمن ثم دفع ثوباً عنه فالشفعة بالثمن لا بالثوب) لأن الثوب عوض عما في ذمة المشتري فيكون البائع مشترياً للثوب بعقد آخر غير العقد الأول، وهذه الحيلة تمنع الجار والشريك لأنه يبتاع العقار بأضعاف قيمته ويعطيه بها ثوباً قدر قيمة العقار غير أنه يخاف أن يتضرر البائع بذلك لأنه لو استحقت الدار تبقى الدراهم كلها في ذمة البائع لوجوبه عليه بالبيع وبراءته حصلت بطريق المقاصة بثمن العقار، فإذا استحق تبين أنه ليس عليه ثمن العقار فبطلت المقاصة فيجب على البائع الثمن كله والحيلة فيه أن يدفع إليه بدل الدراهم الثمن الدنانير بقدر قيمته العقار فيكون صرفاً بما في ذمته من الدارهم، ثم إذا استحق العقار تبين أن لا دین على المشتري فيبطل الصرف للافتراق قبل القبض فيجب رد الدنانير لا غير، والحيلة الأولى تختص بالجوار وهذه لا وحيلة أخرى تعم الجار والشريك أن يشتريه بأضعاف قيمته من الدراهم ثم يوفيه من الدراهم قدر قيمة العقار لا قدر قيمة الدنانير ثم يعطيه الدنانير بالباقي فيصير صرفاً فيه، فإذا استحق المشفوع يرد ما قبض كله فغير الدنانير على أنه بدل عن العقار المستحق والدنيا لبطلان الصرف وإن كان الشفيع خليطاً في نفس المبيع فأراد أن يبيعها من أحدهم وتسقط الشفعة من الباقين فالحيلة فيه أن يجعل الثمن مجهولاً. والصبي والمجنون بمنزلة البالغ في هذه المسألة بعد أن يكون مثل القيمة أو بنقصان يتغابن فيه وهذه حيلة عامة وذكر الخصاف حيلة لم يذكرها محمد وهو أن يدعي أن الدار لابن صغير له في يد هذ ثم إن المدعي يدعي له مائة دينار ولا يقول إنها من ما ابنه الصغير على أنه يسلم الذي في الدار فيجوز ولا شفعة فيها لأن الأب لم يأخذ الدار بطريق المعاوضة، ومن جملة الحيل أن يقر البائع بجزء معلوم من الدار للمشتري ثم يبيع الباقي منه. ومن الحيل أن يوكل المشتري وكيلاً بالشراء فيشتري الوكيل ويغيب ولا يكون الموكل خصماً للشفيع فهذا على قول محمد وعلى قول أبي يوسف يكون خصماً له ا هـ .
For example, he (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he buys it for a price then pays a cloth for it, the pre-emption is by the price, not by the cloth.' Because the cloth is consideration for what is in the buyer's liability, so the seller becomes a buyer of the cloth in another contract different from the first contract. This stratagem prevents the neighbour and the partner, because he buys the property at many times its value and gives for it a cloth worth the value of the property, but he fears that the seller may be harmed because if the property is reclaimed, all the dirhams remain in the seller's liability due to its obligation upon him from the sale, and his discharge occurred by way of set-off with the price of the property. So if it is reclaimed, it becomes clear that there is no price of the property upon him, so the set-off is invalidated, and the entire price is wajib upon the seller. The stratagem in this is that he gives him instead of the dirhams the price in dinars equal to the value of the property, so it becomes a ṣarf (currency exchange) for what is in his liability of dirhams. Then if the property is reclaimed, it becomes clear that there is no debt on the buyer, so the ṣarf is invalidated due to separation before delivery, so only the dinars must be returned. The first stratagem is specific to adjacency, and this one is not; and another stratagem general to both the neighbour and the partner is that he buys it at many times its value in dirhams, then pays him from the dirhams the value of the property not the value of the dinars, then gives him the dinars for the remainder, so it becomes a ṣarf in it. So if the pre-empted property is reclaimed, he returns everything he received except the dinars, because it is a substitute for the reclaimed property, and the dinars are [returned] due to the invalidity of the ṣarf. If the pre-emptor is a shareholder in the very property sold and wants to sell it to one of them and to drop the pre-emption from the rest, the stratagem is that he makes the price unknown. The minor and the insane are in the position of the adult in this issue, provided that it is like the value or a deficiency that is tolerated. This is a general stratagem. Al-Khashshāf mentioned a stratagem that Muhammad did not mention: that he claims that the property belongs to his young son in his hand, then the claimant claims one hundred dinars for him and does not say that it is from his young son's wealth, on the condition that he surrenders what is in the property, and it is permissible and there is no pre-emption because the father did not take the property by way of exchange. Among the stratagems is that the seller acknowledges a known portion of the property for the buyer, then sells the rest to him. Among the stratagems is that the buyer appoints a proxy to buy, so the proxy buys and is absent, and the principal is not a defendant for the pre-emptor. According to Muhammad's view, this is the case; according to Abu Yusuf's view, he is a defendant for him. End.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling. It confirms that if a buyer purchases a property for a price and then pays with a cloth, the pre-emption right is based on the original price, not the cloth. The reason is that the cloth is a separate transaction: the seller is buying the cloth from the buyer. The commentator then discusses various stratagems to avoid pre-emption. One stratagem is to pay with a much larger price in a different currency (gold dinars instead of silver dirhams) to make it a currency exchange (ṣarf). If the property is later reclaimed, the ṣarf becomes void and only the dinars must be returned, protecting the buyer. Other stratagems include making the price unknown, the seller acknowledging a fractional share for the buyer, or having the buyer appoint an absent proxy. The passage also notes that a minor or insane person is treated like an adult in such stratagems. The end mentions a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether the principal is a defendant in pre-emption when an absent proxy is used. The passage is based purely on the text and does not introduce outside rulings.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a partner or neighbour to take possession of a property sold to an outsider by paying the price to the buyer, thereby replacing the buyer in the contract.
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem used to achieve a permissible result while avoiding a legal obligation, such as pre-emption or zakat.
صرف (sarf): The exchange of gold for silver or other currencies, which requires immediate delivery at the contract session.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person buys a house for 1000 dirhams and pays with a cloth worth 1000 dirhams, what is the pre-emption based on?
A) The cloth
B) The price (1000 dirhams)
C) The value of the house
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption is determined by the original price in the sale contract; stratagems that create separate transactions can validly nullify the right of pre-emption, with an intra-Hanafi difference on procedural matters.

Taking Pre-emption from Multiple Buyers vs. Multiple Sellers
Explains the distinction between multiple buyers and multiple sellers in pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking pre-emption from multiple buyers versus multiple sellers and explain the underlying rationale (the principle of avoiding harm to the buyer and the role of the contractor).

Core Idea
Pre-emption can be taken from some buyers but not from some sellers because it would harm the buyer.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentator explains a key distinction in the law of pre-emption (shuf'ah). When there are multiple buyers, the pre-emptor can take the share of one and leave the others. But when there are multiple sellers, he cannot take only part. This asymmetry is rooted in the principle that pre-emption, being an exception to general contract law, should not cause greater harm to the buyer. The passage also addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the ruling changes before or after possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between multiple buyers (one seller) and multiple sellers (one buyer). State the ruling for each: pre-emptor may take from some buyers but not from some sellers.
2
Understand the reasoning
The rationale is that pre-emption is a concession to protect the pre-emptor, but it must not cause extra harm to the buyer by splitting the transaction. In the first case (multiple buyers), taking from some does not harm the buyer because he gets payment from the pre-emptor; in the second case, taking from some forces the buyer into an unwanted partnership.
qiyas: shuf'ah is legislated against analogy to remove harm, so it should not create new harm.
3
Apply the contractor principle
Note that rights of the contract attach to the contractor ('aqid), not the owner. Multiplicity of pre-emption rights is determined by the number of contractors (buyers or sellers), not by ownership. So if a group appoints one agent to buy, the pre-emptor cannot take partial shares.
If multiple agents buy for one principal, multiplicity applies.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Hasan reported from Imam Abu Hanifa that before possession the pre-emptor cannot take from some sellers (due to seller's claim), but after possession he can. The correct opinion (sahih) is that there is no difference because the seller can withhold the property until full payment.
The commentator rejects the riwaya and affirms the unified ruling.
5
Consider related cases
The pre-emptor must pay the full price of the share he takes, even if taking only part. If two properties are bought in one transaction, he must take all or none, unless they are adjacent and his right is only for one.
Unity of transaction (ittihad al-safqah) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وأخذ حظ البعض بتعدد المشتري لا بتعدد البائع) يعني أن المشتري إذا تعدد بأن اشترى جماعة عقاراً والبائع واحد يتعدد الأخذ بالشفعة بتعددهم حتى كان للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم ويترك الباقي، وإن تعدد البائع بأن باع جماعة عقاراً مشتركاً بينهم والمشتري واحد لا يتعدد الأخذ بالشفعة بتعددهم حتى لا يكون للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم دون بعض . والفرق أن الشفيع في الوجه الثاني لو أخذ نصيب بعضهم تتفرق الصفقة على المشتري فيتضرر بعيب الشركة وهي شرعت على خلاف القياس لدفع الضرر عن الشفيع فلا تشرع على وجه يتضرر المشتري ضرراً زائداً على الأخذ بالشفعة، وفي الأول لا تتفرق الصفقة على أحد، ولا هذا أن يكون قبل القبض أو بعده في الصحيح إلا أن الشفيع إذا اختار أخذ فرق في بين الجميع لا يمكنه أن يأخذ نصيب أحدهم إذا نقد حصته من الثمن حتى ينقد الجميع لئلا يؤدي إلى تفريق اليد على البائع بمنزلة المشترين أنفسهم لأنه كواحد منهم، وكما إذا كان المشتري واحداً فنقد البعض من الثمن، وسواء سمى لكل ثمناً أو سمى الكل جملة لأن العبرة في هذا لاتحاد الصفقة لا لاتحاد الثمن واختلافه والعبرة في التعدد والاتحاد للعاقد دون المالك حتى لو وكل أحد جماعة بالشراء فاشتروا له عقاراً واحداً بصفقة واحدة يتعدد وأخذه يتعدد وكان للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب أحدهم، ولو وكل جماعة واحد ليس للشفيع أن يأخذ نصيب بعضهم لأن حقوق العقد تتعلق بالعاقد وهو أصل فيه فيتحدد باتحاده ويتعدد بتعدده . قيدنا بقولنا لا فرق بين أن يكون الأخذ قبل القبض أو بعده في الصحيح وروى الحسن عن الإمام أنه فصل فقال : إن أخذ قبل القبض نصيب أحدهم ليس له ذلك وبعده كان له ذلك لأنه قبل القبض يتضرر البائع بأخذ البعض منه بتفريق اليد عليه وبعده لا يتضرر لأنه لم يبق له يد. وجوابه أن له أن يحبس الجميع إلى أن يستوفي جميع الثمن فلا يؤدي إلى تفريق اليد عليه وإذا اشترى الرجل دارين صفقة واحدة وشفيعها واحد فأراد أن يأخذ أحدهما دون الآخر فليس له ذلك. وفي فتاوى العتابية : ولو كانا متلاصقين وشفيع أحدهما خاصة ولو كانا أرضين أو قرية أو أرضها أو قريتين وأرضهما وهو شفيع ذلك كله فإنما له أن يأخذ جميع ذلك كله فإنما له أن يأخذ جميع ذلك أو يدعه سواء كانا متلاصقين أو في مصرين أو قريتين
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And taking the share of some by multiplicity of buyer, not by multiplicity of seller.' Meaning that if there are multiple buyers (i.e., a group bought a property from one seller), the taking of pre-emption becomes multiple by their multiplicity, so that the pre-emptor may take the share of some and leave the rest. But if there are multiple sellers (i.e., a group sold their jointly owned property to one buyer), the taking of pre-emption does not become multiple by their multiplicity, so the pre-emptor may not take the share of some without others. The difference is that in the second case, if the pre-emptor took the share of some, the single transaction would be fragmented for the buyer, causing him harm due to the defect of partnership. Pre-emption was legislated contrary to analogy to repel harm from the pre-emptor, so it is not legislated in a way that causes additional harm to the buyer. In the first case, the transaction is not fragmented for anyone. And this (ruling) is the same whether the taking is before or after possession in the correct opinion, except that if the pre-emptor chooses to take, he cannot take the share of one of them unless he pays his portion of the price until he pays all of them, so as not to lead to fragmentation of the seller's claim (since he is like one of the buyers). And it is the same as when there is one buyer who pays part of the price. It is the same whether a price is named for each or the total is named in a lump sum, because the consideration is the unity of the transaction, not the unity or difference of the price. The consideration for multiplicity and unity is the contractor, not the owner. So if one person appointed a group to buy and they bought a single property in one transaction, the taking of pre-emption becomes multiple, and the pre-emptor may take the share of one of them. If a group appointed one person to buy, the pre-emptor may not take the share of some, because the rights of the contract are attached to the contractor, who is the principal, so it is unified by his unity and multiple by his multiplicity. We have qualified our statement 'whether the taking is before or after possession in the correct opinion' by noting that al-Hasan reported from Imam Abu Hanifa that he differentiated, saying: if he takes before possession the share of one of them, he does not have that; after possession, he does. Because before possession the seller is harmed by taking only part due to fragmentation of his claim, but after possession he is not harmed because he no longer has a claim. The answer is that he can withhold the entire property until he receives the full price, so it does not lead to fragmentation of the seller's claim. If a man buys two houses in one transaction and there is one pre-emptor for both, and he wants to take one and not the other, he does not have that. In Fatawa al-Atabiyya: if they are adjacent and the pre-emptor is only for one, or if they are lands, a village, its land, two villages, and their lands, and he is the pre-emptor for all that, then he may take all of that or leave it, whether they are adjacent or in two districts or two villages.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage explains a key principle: pre-emption can be exercised selectively when there are multiple buyers, but not when there are multiple sellers. The reason is to avoid harming the buyer by forcing him into a partnership he did not agree to. The passage discusses details: the pre-emptor must pay the full price even if he takes only part; the ruling is the same before or after possession according to the correct view, though Imam Abu Hanifa is reported to distinguish between before and after possession (before possession, the seller would be harmed). The commentator rebuts this by noting the seller can withhold the property until full payment. The passage also addresses multiple properties in one transaction and the pre-emptor can only take all or none.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right to take pre-emption; here discussed in relation to partial taking when there are multiple buyers or sellers.
تعدد (ta'addud): Multiplicity; refers to multiple buyers or sellers in a transaction.
عاقد ('aqid): The contractor; the one who actually makes the contract (the buyer or seller), as opposed to the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If multiple sellers sell a jointly owned property to one buyer, can the pre-emptor take the share of only one seller?
A) Yes
B) No, because it would harm the buyer by forcing him into a partnership
C) Yes, if the price is separate
D) It depends on whether the property is divided

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption can be taken from multiple buyers but not from multiple sellers, to avoid harming the buyer by forcing a partnership.

Shafi' Takes Purchaser's Share of Half an Undivided House
Ruling on shafi' taking share of half-house purchase and division with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a shafi' taking a purchaser's share after division and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the condition of adjacency.

Core Idea
The shafi' takes the purchaser's share at market value and cannot rescind the division, but Abu Hanifa conditions it on adjacency to his property.

When someone buys half an undivided house and then partitions it with the seller, the pre-emptor (shafi') wants to take that share. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the shafi's right and the limits of his power. An important intra-Hanafi dispute arises: does the shafi' have the right to take the share only if it falls adjacent to his own property, or regardless of location? Understanding this nuance is essential for applying shuf'a correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject and Ruling
Determine what the passage is about: a person buys half an undivided house, then divides it with the seller. The question is what the shafi' can do. The ruling: the shafi' takes the purchaser's share by paying its value and cannot rescind the division.
The ruling is clear that the division stands, and the shafi' must respect it.
2
Analyze the Legal Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The passage explains why the shafi' cannot rescind the division: division completes possession (qabd), and the shafi' does not have the right to undo possession to shift liability back to the seller. The division brings complete benefit, so there is no harm (darar) that would justify rescission.
This reasoning ties into the broader principle that shuf'a is a right to remove harm, not to disrupt valid transactions.
3
Map the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Identify the two positions: Imam Abu Hanifa says the shafi' only takes the share if it falls on the side of his own property (adjacent to his house). Abu Yusuf says the shafi' takes it regardless of which side it falls, because the right is based on the purchase, not on location. Note the evidence and the reliance on the absolute wording of the text.
The commentary indicates that the absolute wording of the book (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) supports Abu Yusuf, and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefers that view.
4
Understand Exceptions and Special Cases
The passage also mentions a situation where the shafi' can rescind the division: when the division was not a completion of possession from the original sale, e.g., when two partners buy a house and divide it, then a third shafi' appears. In that case, the division can be rescinded because it was a separate disposition by ownership, not a completion of the first sale.
This exception highlights the principle that the shafi' cannot rescind a division that is 'from the ruling of the first sale' (i.e., part of the qabd).
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the shafi' takes the share regardless of adjacency, and he cannot rescind the division (except in the exceptional case). This is Abu Yusuf's view and is supported by the absolute wording of the matn and the preferred opinion in later Hanafi works.
When issuing fatwa, one should follow this view unless there is specific reason to adopt Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن اشترى نصف دار غير مقسوم أخذ الشفيع حظ المشتري بقيمته) يعني لو اشتری نصف دار غیر مقسوم فقاسم المشتري البائع يأخذ الشفيع نصيب المشتري الذي حصل له بقيمته وليس له أن ينقض القسمة، سواء كانت بقضاء أو تراض، لأن القسمة من تمام القبض لما فيه من تكميل الانتفاع والشفيع لا ينقض القبض ليجعل العهدة على البائع، ولهذا لو باع أو أجر يطيب له الثمن والأجرة وليس للشفيع فيه ملك وإنما له حق الأخذ بالشفعة وذلك لا يمنع نفوذ تصرفاته غير أنه ينقض تصرفاً يبطل حقه لدفع الضرر عن ولا ضرر في القسمة فيبقى على الأصل في حق البيع الأول وفي حق ماله حكمه وهو القبض بجهته، فظاهر عبارة الشارح أنه يأخذه سواء وقع في جانب الدار المشفوع بها أو لا. وفي التجريد عن الأمام أن الشفيع إنما يأخذ النصيب الذي أصاب المشتري إذا وقع في جانب الدار المشفوع بها. وفي واقعات الناطفي أن القسمة إذا كانت بحكم ففي نقض القسمة روايتان. قال الصدر الشهيد في واقعاته والمختار لا نقض بخلاف ما إذا أخذ أحد الشريكين نصيبه من الدار المشتركة وقاسم المشتري الشريك الذي لم يبيع حيث يكون للشفيع نقضه لأن العقد لم يقع من الذي قاسم فلم تكن القسمة من تمام القبض الذي هو حكم البيع الأول بل هو تصرف بحكم الملك فينقضه الشفيع كما ينقض بيعه وهبته وفي التجريد: رجلان اشتريا داراً وهما شفيعان ولهما شفيع ثالث اقتسماها ثم جاء الثالث فله أن ينقض القسمة، سواء اقتسماها بقضاء أو بغير قضاء ا هـ . وأما إذا لم يكن للشفيع نقض القسمة مسألة في الكتاب فيأخذ نصيب المشتري في أي جانب كان لأنه استحقه بالشراء والمشتري لا يقدر على إبطاله فيأخذه وهو قول أبي يوسف وإطلاق الكتاب يدل عليه وقدمنا قول الإمام وإطلاق الماتن صادق على ما إذا قاسم البائع أو غيره وليس كذلك، فلو زاد أو قاسم البائع لسلم من الاعتراض اهـ.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If someone buys half a house that is undivided, the shafi' takes the purchaser's share by its value.) That is, if someone buys half an undivided house and the purchaser divides it with the seller, the shafi' takes the share that the purchaser obtained by its value, and he does not have the right to rescind the division, whether it was by court order or mutual consent, because the division completes the possession due to completing the benefit, and the shafi' does not rescind the possession to make the liability fall on the seller. For this reason, if he sells or rents, the price and rental are permissible for him, and the shafi' has no ownership in it; rather, he has the right of shuf'a. That does not prevent the validity of his dispositions, except that he rescinds a disposition that nullifies his right to repel harm, but there is no harm in the division, so it remains on the original basis regarding the first sale and regarding what has its ruling, which is possession by its means. The apparent statement of the commentator is that he takes it whether it falls on the side of the house subject to shuf'a or not. In al-Tajreed, from al-Imam, that the shafi' only takes the share that the purchaser obtained if it falls on the side of the house subject to shuf'a. In Waqi'at al-Natafi, regarding the division if it is by ruling, there are two narrations about rescinding the division. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said in his Waqi'at: The chosen position is that there is no rescission, contrary to the case where one of the two partners takes his share from the shared house and the purchaser divides with the partner who did not sell; then the shafi' has the right to rescind it, because the contract did not occur from the one who divided, so the division is not a completion of the possession that is the ruling of the first sale; rather, it is a disposition by virtue of ownership, so the shafi' rescinds it just as he rescinds his sale and gift. In al-Tajreed: Two men bought a house and they are shafi's, and they have a third shafi'; they divided it, then the third came; he has the right to rescind the division, whether they divided by court order or without. End. As for the case where the shafi' does not have the right to rescind the division, it is an issue in the book: he takes the purchaser's share in whichever side it is, because he acquired it by purchase, and the purchaser cannot nullify it, so he takes it. This is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and the absolute wording of the book indicates it. We have previously stated the saying of al-Imam, and the absolute wording of the author applies to the case where he divides with the seller or otherwise, but it is not like that. So if he increased or divided with the seller, he is safe from objection. End.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It addresses the situation where a person buys half of a house that is undivided (i.e., not partitioned). After the purchase, the buyer divides the property with the seller, meaning they partition the house so each gets a specific portion. The question is: can the shafi' (the person with the right of pre-emption) take the share that the purchaser obtained? The ruling: the shafi' takes that share, but he must pay its value (not the price the purchaser paid, but the market value at the time of taking). He cannot rescind the division, because the division is considered a completion of the possession (qabd) that occurred by the sale. The shafi' does not have the right to undo the possession to make the seller liable again. However, there is a difference of opinion among the Hanafi imams. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the shafi' only takes the share if it falls on the side of the shafi's own property (the house adjacent to which he has the right of pre-emption). According to Abu Yusuf, the shafi' takes wherever the share falls, because the shafi' acquires it by virtue of the purchase, and the purchaser cannot nullify it. The commentary also discusses the case where the division is made by a court order versus mutual consent, and the ruling of al-Sadr al-Shahid that generally there is no rescission of the division, except in a specific scenario where one partner took his share and the purchaser divided with the other partner—then the shafi' can rescind. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that there is no rescission, and the shafi' takes the share regardless of side, as per Abu Yusuf's view.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of a partner to take the sold property by paying its price.
قسمة (qisma): Division of jointly owned property, here partition of a house into shares.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery of property; here considered completed by division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what condition must be met for the shafi' to take the purchaser's share after division?
A) The share must fall on the side adjacent to the shafi's property.
B) The division must be by mutual consent, not court order.
C) The shafi' must pay the price the purchaser paid.
D) There is no condition of adjacency; the shafi' takes the share wherever it falls.

Key Takeaway
The shafi' takes the purchaser's share after division at market value and cannot rescind the division, with the mufta bihi allowing it regardless of adjacency.

Shuf'a Rights of a Ma'dhun Slave and His Master
Ruling on shuf'a between ma'dhun slave and master conditioned on debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a ma'dhun slave may exercise shuf'a against his master and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas) that validates it.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave with overwhelming debt can claim shuf'a from his master because the debt severs the master's ownership over the slave's assets.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuanced scenario in sales law: the mutual shuf'a rights between a master and his ma'dhun slave. The general rule that a slave cannot transact with his master is suspended when the slave is burdened by debt that exceeds his value. The commentator uses qiyas to analogize shuf'a to purchase, and then delineates the conditions. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the role of debt in altering legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text states that the ma'dhun slave may take shuf'a from his master, and vice versa, but the condition that the slave must have overwhelming debt is implicit and later emphasized by the commentator.
The matn appears general; the commentary restricts it.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is that shuf'a is like a purchase (mubadala mal bi mal). Since a purchase between master and indebted slave is valid, shuf'a is also valid.
This is a qiyas jaliy: asl = validity of purchase, far' = validity of shuf'a.
3
Analyze the qiyas
The asl is the validity of a purchase when the slave has debt (because the slave acquires milk al-yad). The illa is the similarity between shuf'a and purchase. The condition of debt ensures the slave has separate legal capacity.
The commentator notes that without debt, purchase is invalid, so shuf'a also falls.
4
Examine the reverse scenario
When the ma'dhun slave is the seller, the master may also take shuf'a. This is because the slave's sale to the master is valid only if the slave has debt; otherwise, the sale is invalid and no shuf'a arises.
The same condition of debt applies symmetrically.
5
Note the commentator's refinement
The commentator suggests that restricting the ruling to the indebted slave (al-madyun) is more precise, emphasizing that debt is the essential condition for the validity of the transaction and thus the shuf'a right.
This refinement limits the general wording of the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وللعبد المأذون الأخذ بالشفعة من سيده كعكسه) يعني إذا باع رجل داراً وللبائع عبد مأذون له في التجارة وعليه دين يحيط برقبته وماله فللعبد أن يأخذ الدار بالشفعة، وكذا عكسه وهو ما إذا كان العبد المأذون هو البائع فلمولاه الأخذ بالشفعة. لأن الأخذ بالشفعة بمنزلة الشراء، وشراء أحدهما من صاحبه جائز إذا كان على العبد دين لأنه يفيد ملك اليد للعبد لكون المولى لا يملك ما في يد عبده المديون أو لكون العبد أحق به بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن عليه دين والعبد بائع لأنه بيعه لمولاه ولا شفعة لمن يبيع له بخلاف ما إذا اشترى لأنه ابتيع له وقد بينا أن من ابتاع أو ابتيع له لا تبطل شفعته، ولو قيد بالمديون لكان أولى .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (The ma'dhun slave may take the property by shuf'a from his master, and vice versa.) That is, if a man sells a house and the seller has a ma'dhun slave who has permission to trade and is burdened with debt that encircles his value and wealth, then the slave may take the house by shuf'a. Similarly, the reverse: if the ma'dhun slave is the seller, then his master may take by shuf'a. Because taking by shuf'a is like buying, and the purchase of one from the other is valid if the slave has debt, because it gives the slave possession (yad) since the master does not own what is in the hand of his indebted slave, or because the slave is more entitled to it. Contrary to the case where there is no debt and the slave is the seller, because his sale to his master is not valid, and there is no shuf'a for one who sells to him. Contrary to the case where he buys, because he is buying for him. We have explained that one who buys or for whom a purchase is made does not lose his right of shuf'a. If it were restricted to the indebted slave, it would be better.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage discusses the rights of shuf'a involving a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade). The key ruling: a ma'dhun slave who is heavily in debt (such that his debt exceeds his value and assets) may exercise the right of shuf'a to take a house from his master. Conversely, if the ma'dhun slave is the seller of a house, then the master may exercise shuf'a to take it from the slave. The reasoning is that taking by shuf'a is analogous to buying (mubadala mal bi mal). Normally, a slave cannot buy from his master because the slave's property belongs to the master. However, when the slave has overwhelming debt, his property is effectively for the creditors, and the master loses ownership over the assets in the slave's hand. Therefore, the purchase (and thus shuf'a) is valid. If the slave has no debt, the ruling is different: if the slave sells to the master, the sale is invalid, and the master has no shuf'a because he is the seller's own master. If the slave buys from the master, the sale is also invalid without debt. The commentator suggests that restricting the ruling to the indebted slave is more precise.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
ملك اليد (milk al-yad): Possession without full ownership; the slave has possession for the benefit of creditors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can a ma'dhun slave take a house by shuf'a from his master?
A) Always, the slave has full rights.
B) Only if the slave has no debts.
C) Only if the slave has debts that exceed his value and wealth.
D) Never, a slave cannot take from his master.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's right of shuf'a against his master exists only when the slave is burdened with debt that exceeds his assets, because debt severs the master's ownership and gives the slave independent capacity.

Guardian's Surrender of Shuf'a for a Minor
Ruling and ikhtilaf on guardian's right to waive minor's shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a guardian's surrender of shuf'a for a minor and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is like trade; a guardian may surrender it for a minor unless it causes pure harm.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: can a father or executor validly waive the shuf'a right of a minor? The matn declares it valid, but Muhammad and Zufar dissent. Understanding their arguments reveals the school's approach to guardianship and the nature of shuf'a as either trade or harm-repelling right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of the matn
The matn states that the surrender of shuf'a from the father, executor, and agent is valid. This is the base position.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the surrender is valid. Muhammad and Zufar hold that the minor retains the shuf'a until puberty and the guardian cannot surrender it.
This is an ikhtilaf within the school, not between madhhabs.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Muhammad and Zufar argue that shuf'a is legislated to repel harm, so surrendering it causes harm, analogous to pardoning qisas or freeing a slave without compensation.
The opposing view relies on the principle that guardians cannot cause pure harm to the minor.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Imam and Abu Yusuf respond that shuf'a taking is essentially trade (exchange of property), and the guardian has authority over the minor's trade. Surrendering is not pure harm; it may benefit the minor by avoiding liability.
The Hanafi response emphasizes the trade-like nature of shuf'a, allowing the guardian discretion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وصح تسليمهم الشفعة من الأب والوصي والوكيل) يعني أن الحمل والصغير في استحقاق الشفعة كالكبير لاستوائهما في سببه فيقوم بالطلب والأخذ والتسلم من يقوم مقامهما وهو الأب ثم وصيه ثم أب الأب ثم وصيه ثم الوصي الذي نصبه القاضي، فإن لم يكن أحد فهو على شفعته حتى يدرك، وهذا قول الأمام وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد وزفر : هو على شفعته إذا بلع. وعلى هذا الخلاف بطلان الشفعة بسكوت الأب والوصي عند العلم بالشراء؛ للإمام محمد وزفر أن هذا إبطال لحق الصبي فلا يصح كالعفو عن القود وإعتاق عبده وإبراء عن يمينه ولأن تصرفهما نظري والنظر في الأخذ يتعين ألا ترى أنه شرع لدفع الضرر فكان في إبطاله إلحاق الضرر به فلا يملك ولهما أن الأخذ بالشفعة في معنى التجارة بل هو عينها ألا أنه مبادلة المال بالمال وترك الأخذ بها ترك التجارة فيملكه تری كما يملك ترك التجارة يوضحه أنه لو أخذه بالشفعة ثم باعه من ذلك الرجل بعينه جاز فكذا إذا سلمه إليه بل أولى لأنه إذا أخذه ثم باعه من ذلك الرجل بعينه جاز كانت العهدة على الصبي، وفي الأول على البائع أو المشتري ولأن هذا تصرف دائر بين النفع والضرر فيحتمل أن يكون الترك أنفع بايفاء الثمن على ملك الصبي بخلاف العفو عن القود وما ذكر معه لأنه ضرر محض غير متردد، ولأنه إبطال بغير عوض. هذا إذا بيعت بمثل قيمتها، وإن بيعت بأكثر من قيمتها بما لا يتغابن الناس في مثله ؛ قيل جاز التسليم بالإجماع لأن النظر متعين فيه، وقيل لا يجوز التسليم بالإجماع وهو الأصح لأنه لا يملك الأخذ فلا يملك التسليم كالأجنبي، وإن بيعت بأقل من قيمتها بمحاباة كثيرة فعند الإمام لا يصح تسليم الأب والوصي ولا رواية عن أبي يوسف قال في النهاية : ولما لم يصح التسليم على قول الإمام لا يصح على قول محمد وزفر بالأولى، ولو كان المشتري هو الأب لنفسه كان له أن يأخذه بالشفعة ما لم يكن فيه ضرر ظاهر على الصغير وكذا لو اشترى لابنه الصغير كان له أن يأخذ بالشفعة ما لم يكن فيه ضرر ظاهر وهو أن لا يكون فيه غبن فاحش فكذا في هذا إلا أنه يشترط في حقه أن يكون فيه نفع بالصغير ظاهر حتى إذا كان كالأب في بمثل القيمة لا يجوز. وكذا إذا باع من نفسه بمثل القيمة لا يجوز حتى يكون أكثر، وفي الأب يجوز إذا كان بمثل القيمة فيهما. ثم كيفية طلبه أن يقول اشتريت وأخذت بالشفعة والوصي الأخذ،
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And their surrender of shuf'a from the father, executor, and agent is valid). That is, the fetus and minor are like the adult in entitlement to shuf'a, since they are equal in its cause. So whoever represents them (the father, then his executor, then the grandfather, then his executor, then the executor appointed by the judge) undertakes the request, taking, and surrender. If there is no one, he remains on his shuf'a until he matures. This is the saying of al-Imam (Abu Hanifa) and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and Zufar said: He remains on his shuf'a until he attains puberty. Upon this difference rests the nullification of shuf'a by the silence of the father and executor when they learn of the purchase. According to Imam Muhammad and Zufar, this is a nullification of the minor's right, so it is not valid, like the pardon for retribution, emancipation of his slave, and discharge of his oath. Moreover, their action is considered in the interest of the minor, and the interest in taking is certain; do you not see that it is legislated to repel harm, so in its nullification there is causing harm to him, thus they do not have the authority. For them (Imam and Abu Yusuf), taking by shuf'a is in the nature of trade; indeed it is precisely trade, as it is an exchange of property for property, and abandoning it is abandoning trade, so they have the authority, just as they have authority to abandon trade. This is clarified by the fact that if he takes it by shuf'a and then sells it to that same man, it is valid; similarly, if he surrenders it to him, even more so. Because if he takes it and then sells it to that same man, the liability falls on the minor, whereas in the first case it falls on the seller or purchaser. And this action (surrender) oscillates between benefit and harm, so it is possible that leaving it is more beneficial in preserving the price on the minor's property, unlike the pardon for retribution and what was mentioned with it, because that is pure harm without hesitation. Moreover, it is nullification without compensation. This applies if it was sold at its fair price. If it was sold at more than its price by an amount not usually overlooked, it was said that the surrender is valid by consensus because the benefit is certain. And it was said that it is not valid by consensus, and this is the more correct, because he does not have the authority to take, so he does not have the authority to surrender, like a stranger. And if it was sold at less than its price with significant favoritism, then according to Imam the surrender of the father and executor is not valid, and there is no narration from Abu Yusuf. Al-Nihaya said: Since the surrender is not valid according to Imam, it is even more so not valid according to Muhammad and Zufar. If the buyer is the father for himself, he may take it by shuf'a as long as there is no apparent harm to the minor. Similarly, if he buys for his minor son, he may take by shuf'a as long as there is no apparent harm, i.e., no excessive loss. So too in this case, except that it is conditioned in his right that there be apparent benefit to the minor, so if it is like the father in terms of fair price, it is not valid. And if he sells to himself at fair price, it is not valid unless it is more. And for the father, it is valid if it is at fair price in both cases. Then the manner of his request: he says, 'I have bought and taken by shuf'a.' And the executor takes.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage discusses whether a guardian (father, executor, agent) can validly surrender (taslim) the right of shuf'a on behalf of a minor or fetus. The matn states that such surrender is valid. The commentary explains that the fetus and minor have the right of shuf'a just like an adult, and the guardian acts for them. If there is no guardian, the right remains until maturity. There is a significant intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the guardian can surrender, because taking by shuf'a is akin to trade (exchange of property), and the guardian can engage in or abandon trade for the minor. If surrendering is beneficial (e.g., avoiding loss), it is allowed. The opposing view of Muhammad and Zufar is that the guardian cannot surrender because shuf'a is a right that repels harm, and surrendering it would harm the minor, similar to pardoning qisas or freeing a slave, which are pure harm and not in the minor's interest. The text then discusses scenarios: if the property is sold at a fair price, the issue is disputed. If sold at a significantly higher price, there are two opinions: one says surrender is valid by consensus because the minor clearly benefits, another says it is not valid because the guardian cannot take such a right. The more correct opinion is that it is not valid. If sold at a lower price with favoritism (mahabah), the surrender is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and it is even more invalid according to the opposing view. Additional rules: the father can take by shuf'a for himself or his son as long as there is no apparent harm (e.g., excessive loss). The procedure for taking is to say 'I have bought and taken by shuf'a.'

Key Terms
تسليم (taslim): Waiving the right of shuf'a, usually by a guardian for a minor.
حمل (haml): Fetus in the womb; considered to have the right of shuf'a.
نظري (nazari): Pertaining to the guardian's duty to act in the minor's best interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, can a father validly surrender the right of shuf'a for his minor child?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, never.
C) Yes, because shuf'a is considered trade.
D) No, because it harms the minor.

Key Takeaway
A guardian may validly surrender the minor's shuf'a because shuf'a is akin to trade, and the guardian has authority over the minor's trade; however, surrender is invalid if it constitutes pure harm, such as when the property is sold at a significantly undervalued price.

The Dual Nature of Division: Ifrāz and Mubādalah
Explains the legal nature of division as a hybrid of separation and exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual nature of division (qismah) in Hanafi law as a combination of separation (ifrāz) and exchange (mubādalah), and apply this distinction to determine rulings such as taking a share in the absence of a co-owner.

Core Idea
Division is never purely separation nor exchange; both coexist, but one dominates depending on property type.

Division (qismah) of jointly owned property might seem straightforward, but Hanafi jurists analyzed it deeply. They found that division always contains two elements: ifrāz (separation, taking one's own) and mubādalah (exchange, swapping parts). Which aspect dominates affects practical rules—like whether a co-owner can take his share without the other being present. This lesson unpacks that analysis and its consequences, drawing on the text from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of division
Recognize that the author introduces qismah as a composite act: إفراز (separation) and مبادلة (exchange). In fungibles (mithliyyāt), ifrāz is dominant; in non-fungibles (qimiyyāt), mubādalah is apparent.
2
Classify property types and their ruling
Distinguish mithlī (fungible) from ghayr mithlī (non-fungible). In mithlī, a co-owner can take his share in the absence of the other because the shares are exactly alike (ifrāz). In ghayr mithlī, he cannot because the exchange aspect requires both parties' presence.
3
Trace the practical implications for contracts
The author shows that if division were pure exchange, contracts like qard (loan), ṣarf (currency exchange), and salam (forward sale) would be invalid under certain conditions. Because it partakes of ifrāz, those contracts remain valid. Identify the specific reasoning.
4
Analyze the objection about wine division
The text raises an objection: if division of wine (considered impure) is disliked because of exchange, but taking possession of wine for a dhimmi is permissible, why is division disliked? The response clarifies that when only wine exists, the division is pure ifrāz (no exchange), so no dislike. When pigs are included, exchange appears, causing dislike.
5
Synthesize the overall principle
Understand the final conclusion: division always involves both ifrāz and mubādalah from different perspectives. The relative dominance determines the ruling, but both aspects are always present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة طلب أحد الشركاء لا في غيره وندب نصب قاسم رزقه في بيت المال ليقسم بلا أجر ذوات الأمثال حتى كان لأحد الشريكين أن يأخذ نصيبه حال غيبة صاحبه، والمبادلة هي الظاهر في غير المثلي كالثياب والعقار والحيوان حتى لا يأخذ نصيبه حال غيبة صاحبه وإن كان معنى الإفراز ظاهراً في المثلي لأن ما يأخذ كل واحد منهما مثل حقه صورة ومعنى فأمكن أن يجعل عين حقه في القرض والصرف والسلم لأنه لو كان مبادلة لما صح في القرض قبض للافتراق قبل أحد العوضين ولا في الصرف والسلم لحرمة الاستبدال فيهما. قال في النهاية : فإن قلت ليس أن محمداً ذكر كتاب القسمة إذا . كان وصي الذمي مسلماً وفي التركة خمور أنه يكره قسمتهما، ولو كان الرجحان في هذه القسمة للإفراز ينبغي أن يجوز من غير كراهة فإن الذمي إذا وكل مسلماً أن يقبض خمراً له جاز قبضها من غير كراهة . قلت : ذكر شمس الأئمة الحلواني إذا كان في التركة خمور لا يكره للوصي المسلم قسمتها لأن هذا إفراز محض ليس فيه شبهة المبادلة وإنما تكره القسمة إذا كان مع الخمر الخنازير لأن القسمة حينئذ تكون مبادلة، وغيره من المشايخ قالوا : لا بل يكره قسمة الخمور وحدها لأن العمل بالشبهين في قسمة الخمور وحدها يمكن إثبات الكراهة، ومعنى الكراهة هنا هو ما بين الحلال المطلق والحرام المحض، وإنما كان معنى المبادلة في غير المثلي أظهر للتفاوت فلا يمكن أن يجعل كأنه لعدم المعادلة بينهما بيقين. ولو اشترى داراً فاقتسماها لا يبيع مرابحة بعد القسمة ولك أن تقول إن القسمة لا تعرى عن معنى الإفراز والمبادلة في جميع الصور سواء كانت في ذوات الأمثال أو في غيره لأنها بالنظر إلى البعض إفراز بالنظر إلى البعض الآخر مبادلة، وإذا كان كذلك فغاية الأمر أن البعض الذي يأخذه كل منهما عوض مما في يد صاحبه وليس بمثل له بيقين فلم يتحقق معنى الإفراز فيه بالنظر إلى ذلك البعض فلا يلزم فيه أن لا يتحقق الإفراز فيه إلى البعض الآخر وهو كونه بعض حقه في الجملة فثبتت المساواة بين المبادلة والافراز غير أن الظهور للمبادلة .
Book of Division: The demand of one of the partners, not in other than it, and the recommendation to appoint a divider whose sustenance is in the treasury so that he divides without fee.  [Regarding] fungible properties, it is such that for one of the two partners to take his share during the absence of his partner is permissible. And the exchange aspect is apparent in non-fungible properties such as clothes, real estate, and animals, so they cannot take his share during the absence of his partner, although the meaning of separation is apparent in fungibles because each one takes the like of his right in form and meaning, so it is possible to make it as if he is taking the very thing that is his right. This has an effect in [the contracts of] qard, sarf, and salam, because if it were an exchange, then in qard, taking possession before separation from one of the two countervalues would not be valid, nor in sarf and salam due to the prohibition of substitution in them. He said in al-Nihayah: 'If you say: Did not Muhammad mention the Book of Division when the executor of a dhimmi is Muslim and there are wines in the estate that he dislikes their division? And if the preponderance in this division is for separation, it should be permissible without dislike, for when a dhimmi appoints a Muslim to take possession of his wine, it is permissible for him to take it without dislike. I say: Shams al-Aimmah al-Halwani mentioned that when there are wines in the estate, it is not disliked for the Muslim executor to divide them because this is pure separation with no resemblance to exchange; the division is only disliked if there are pigs along with the wine because then the division becomes an exchange. Other scholars said: No, rather the division of wine alone is disliked because acting on both probabilities in the division of wine alone can establish dislike, and the meaning of dislike here is what is between absolute halal and pure haram. And the meaning of exchange is more apparent in non-fungibles due to disparity, so it cannot be made as if it were not for the certain lack of equivalence between them. And if one buys a house and they divide it, he cannot sell it as murabahah after division. But you may say: Division is never devoid of the meaning of separation and exchange in all forms, whether in fungible or non-fungible things, because from one perspective it is separation and from another it is exchange. If that is so, then the utmost is that the portion each takes is a substitute for what is in the hand of his partner, and it is not exactly like it with certainty, so the meaning of separation is not realized with respect to that portion; it does not necessitate that separation is not realized with respect to another portion, namely it being part of his right in general. Thus equality between exchange and separation is established, but exchange is more apparent.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This is an explanatory passage about the legal nature of division (qismah). The author explains that division partakes of two elements: إفراز (separation) and مبادلة (exchange). In fungible properties (mithliyyat) like wheat or oil, the separation aspect dominates, so a co-owner can take his share even in the absence of the other. In non-fungible properties (qimiyyat) like clothes or real estate, the exchange aspect is stronger because the shares are not exactly identical, so taking the share absent the other co-owner is not allowed. The difference has practical implications: e.g., in contracts like qard, sarf, and salam, if division were pure exchange, certain conditions would not be met, but since it's partly separation, these contracts remain valid. The text then discusses an objection about dividing wines when a Muslim executor is involved: some scholars dislike it due to resemblance to exchange, others permit it as pure separation. The objection is resolved by noting that division always has both aspects, but exchange is more apparent in non-fungibles. A concluding example: if two buy a house and divide it, they cannot resell it as murabahah without informing the buyer because the division introduced an exchange element.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): The act of dividing jointly owned property among co-owners, legally analyzed as a combination of separation (إفراز) and exchange (مبادلة).
إفراز (ifrāz): The aspect of division where each co-owner simply takes his own right without exchanging anything, applicable in fungible properties.
مبادلة (mubādalah): The aspect of division where co-owners exchange portions of the property to achieve separate ownership, dominant in non-fungible properties.
مثلي (mithlī): A property that has standard market equivalents, such as weighed or measured goods; in division, the shares can be exactly alike.
غير مثلي (ghayr mithlī): A property without standard equivalents, like real estate, animals, and clothes; division necessarily involves exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which type of property can a co-owner take his share during the absence of the other co-owner according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) In fungible properties (mithliyyat)
B) In non-fungible properties (qimiyyat)
C) In both types
D) In real estate only

Key Takeaway
Division (qismah) in Hanafi law is inherently dual: it always combines separation (ifrāz) and exchange (mubādalah), but which aspect dominates determines key practical rules, such as the permissibility of taking shares in a co-owner's absence.

Forced Division in Homogeneous Property: The Ruling and Its Analogical Proof
States the ruling on forced division in homogeneous property and provides an analogical proof (qiyas) based on harm prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forced division in homogeneous property and explain the qiyas proof based on harm prevention.

Core Idea
A co-owner can be compelled to divide only in homogeneous property; compulsion is justified to prevent harm.

This lesson examines the core ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: that a partner can compel division only when the property is of the same genus. We will analyze the proof via qiyas, addressing an objection that division involves exchange. Understanding this ruling is essential for handling joint ownership disputes in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the matn statement: 'And he is compelled in the homogenous genus upon the request of one of the partners, not in other than it.' Recognize that forced division applies only when the property is homogeneous (same genus).
Homogeneity is the key condition; fungibility is not required.
2
Understand the objection
An anonymous objector argues that division involves an exchange of property, and exchange cannot be compelled. Identify this objection in the passage's citation from al-Nihayah.
The objector's point is that forced exchange violates the principle of mutual consent.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response via qiyas
The response analogizes to a debtor who is imprisoned until his property is sold to pay a debt. The effective cause (illa) is the prevention of harm (daf' al-darar). Apply this analogy to the co-owner's refusal to divide.
The qiyas establishes that compulsion for harm prevention is legally valid.
4
Note the practical consequence
Because the compulsion is for harm prevention, the rules of deception (ghurur) do not apply. If one partner builds on his share and it turns out the property was claimed by another, he cannot claim compensation from the other partner.
This consequence is derived from the nature of the compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ويجبر في متحد الجنس عند طلب أحد الشركاء لا في غيره) يعني إذا طلب بعض الشركاء القسمة يجبر الآبي على القسمة في متحد الجنس، سواء كان من ذوات الأمثال أو لا، ولا يجبر في غير متحد الجنس كالغنم مع الإبل لما بينا من المعنى. وفي غاية البيان قال في الفتاوى الصغرى القسمة ثلاثة أنواع : قسمة لا يجبر الآبي عليها كقسمة الأجناس المختلفة، وقسمة يجبر عليها الآبي كقسمة ذوات الأمثال كالمكيل والموزون، وقسمة يجبر الآبي في غير المثليات كالثياب من نوع واحد والبقر والغنم. والخيارات ثلاثة: خيار شرط، وخيار رؤية وخيار عيب. ففي قسمة الأجناس المختلفة تثبت الخيارات الثلاث، وفي قسمة ذوات الأمثال كالمكيل يثبت خيار العيب دون خيار الشرط والرؤية، فخيار الرؤية والعيب يثبتان من غر شرط بخلاف خيار الشرط. وفي قسمة الثياب من نوع واحد والبقر والغنم يثبت خيار العيب وهل يثبت خيار الرؤية؟ على رواية أبي سليمان يثبت وهو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى ويثبت فيه خيار العيب من غير خلاف ا هـ . وفي الذخيرة : القاضي لا يقسم الأجناس المختلفة قسمة إذا أبى بعض الشركاء بأن كان بينهم إبل وغنم وطلب جمع أحدهم من القاضي أن يجمع نصيبه في الإبل والبقر والغنم فالقاضي لا يقسم على هذا الوجه، وفي الجنس المتحد يقسم قسمة جمع عند طلب البعض بأن كان بينهم غنم كثيرة أو إبل كثيرة وطلب أحدهم من القاضي أن يجمع نصيبه في طائفة منها فعل القاضي ذلك ا هـ . وفي النهاية : اعترض على قوله يجبر بأن المبادلة معتبرة فيها فكيف يجبر؟ وأجيب بأنه يجبر لدفع الضرر عن غيره كالغريم يحبس حتى يباع ماله لبعض الدين ولهذا لا يثبت حكم الغرور فيها حتى لو أخذ أحدهما الدار وبنى في نصيبه فاستحق الدار التي بنى فيها لا يرجع على صاحبه بقيمة بنائه إذا نقض ا هـ . وظاهر العبارة صادق بطلب صاحب القليل والكثير وسيأتي تقييده.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And he is compelled in the homogenous genus upon the request of one of the partners, not in other than it) meaning: if some of the partners request division, the refusing partner is compelled to division in homogenous genus, whether it is of the fungible type or not; he is not compelled in non-homogenous genus like sheep with camels, for the meaning we have explained. And in Ghayat al-Bayan, he said in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: Division is of three types: a division in which the refuser is not compelled, like division of different genera; a division in which the refuser is compelled, like division of fungibles such as quantities weighed and measured; and a division in which the refuser is compelled in non-fungibles like clothes of one type, cows, and sheep. And the options are three: option of stipulation, option of sight, and option of defect. In division of different genera, all three options are established. In division of fungibles like weighed items, the option of defect is established without the option of stipulation and sight. The options of sight and defect are established without a stipulation, unlike the option of stipulation. In division of clothes of one type, cows, and sheep, the option of defect is established, and as for whether the option of sight is established? According to the narration of Abu Sulayman, it is established, and this is the correct view and upon it is the fatwa, and the option of defect is established without dispute, end quote. And in al-Dhakhirah: The judge does not divide different genera as a division if some partners refuse, such as if there are camels and sheep between them and one of them asks the judge to combine his share in camels, cows, and sheep, the judge does not divide in this manner. In the homogenous genus, he divides as a combined division upon request of some, such as if there are many sheep or many camels between them and one of them asks the judge to combine his share in a group of them, the judge does that, end quote. And in al-Nihayah: An objection was raised to his saying 'he is compelled' that exchange is considered in it, so how can he be compelled? And it was answered: He is compelled to remove harm from others, like a debtor is imprisoned until his property is sold for part of the debt. For this reason, the ruling of deception is not established in it, so much so that if one of them took the house and built in his share, then the house in which he built was claimed by another, he does not have recourse against his partner for the value of his building if it is demolished, end quote. And the apparent wording applies to the request of the one with little and the one with much, and its restriction will come later.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on forced division (جبر) from the matn of كنز الدقائق. The key rule: if the property is homogeneous (متحد الجنس) — meaning all items are of the same genus — a partner can compel the other(s) to divide, whether the property is fungible or non-fungible. In heterogeneous property (different genera), such as camels mixed with sheep, no one can be forced to divide. The commentary then elaborates by quoting al-Fatawa al-Sughra, which classifies division into three types: (1) division not forced (different genera), (2) division forced in fungibles, (3) division forced in non-fungibles of one type. It also explains how the three options (خيارات) apply: in different genera, all three options exist; in fungibles, only the option of defect; in non-fungibles of one type, both option of defect and, according to the sound position, option of sight apply. The text then cites al-Dhakhirah on how a judge handles requests for collective division (قسمة جمع) in homogeneous herds. Finally, it addresses an objection from al-Nihayah: if division involves exchange, how can one be compelled? The answer is that compulsion is for harm prevention, analogous to imprisoning a debtor to sell his property, and therefore the effect of deceit (غرور) does not apply—meaning if one partner builds on his share and it turns out the property was not fully owned, he cannot claim compensation from the other.

Key Terms
متحد الجنس (muttaḥid al-jins): Property that belongs to the same genus, e.g., sheep with sheep, gold with gold; forced division applies.
جبر (jabr): Legal compulsion upon a renuant co-owner to accept division when one partner requests it in homogeneous property.
خيار (khiyār): The right to cancel or confirm a transaction upon discovering a defect, after sight, or as per stipulation; applied here to division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for allowing compulsion in division?
A) To ensure equal ownership
B) To prevent harm
C) To facilitate trade
D) To respect the judge's authority

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, forced division is justified only in homogeneous property to prevent harm, analogized to forcing a debtor to sell his property.
